Distinguishing 'Azima and Rukhsah: Necessity Does Not Abrogate Liability
Discussion of 'azima and rukhsah with examples of necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that rukhsah permits a prohibited act under necessity but retains the obligation to compensate or pay a penalty.

Core Idea
Rukhsah permits the prohibited in necessity, but liability remains—dispensation does not abrogate the original obligation.

This passage from al-Pazdawi's Usul clarifies the relationship between the original ruling ('azima) and the dispensation (rukhsah). It emphasizes that foundational obligations like faith and prayer cannot be dropped, while other rulings may be relaxed under necessity but often still require compensation. The example of a starving person taking another's food illustrates that permission is not absolute.

Guided Path
1
Identify 'azima and rukhsah
Locate where the passage distinguishes the original binding ruling ('azima) from the dispensation (rukhsah). Note that 'azima applies normally, while rukhsah is a concession for necessity.
e.g., 'azima is like the basic obligation to pray; rukhsah is like combining prayers while traveling.
2
Recognize exceptions to rukhsah
Find the statement that some rulings do not tolerate dispensation—like tawhid, iman, and adā' (performance of prayer). These are pillars of the Sharia that cannot be abandoned.
These are 'umad al-shar' (pillars of law) and 'asl al-din (foundation of religion).
3
Analyze the famine example
Work through the example of a person with no food who finds another's food. The passage says there is no 'ibāḥa muṭlaqa (absolute permission). If he takes it, he must compensate; if he refrains and dies, he is a martyr.
This shows that necessity does not suspend the right of the owner; liability remains.
4
Extend to similar cases
Apply the same logic to eating from the haram (e.g., game in the sanctuary). Necessity permits the act but imposes a penalty (jaza').
The pattern: permission with compensation/penalty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في العقيلة ضَرُورَةُ لَمْ تَحْتمَل الرَّحْصَلَة بِالدِيلِ وَوَخَلَتَ الرخصة فى الاداء لِلصَّورَةِ وَلِمَا سَبَقَ انَّ الامل في التشريع التوحيد والإيمان والاصل في الاعتقاد والآداء فيه ركن مو اليه فصَارَتْ عُمَكَ الشرع وأساس الدين الذي لا تحتمل السقوط وَالتَعَدّى من المُسْرَ عَيْلِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَصَارَ غيرُهُ عَرَمَة . العَارِضِ وَمَا كَانَ من حقوقِ العِبَادِ وَمِن جلس مَا حَتَ بَل السقوط من حُقوقِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى قسم آخر انه يحتمل السعوط بِأَصْلِهِ لَكن وَلِيلُ العُمَوطِ لَمَّا لَمْ يُوجَدٌ وَمَا رَضَهُ أَمْرُ فَوْقَهُ وَجَبَ العَمليه بإثباتِ الرَّحْصَةِ وَالمَّا وَجَبَ بِاصْلِهِ بِأَنْ جُعِل اَصْلُهُ عَزِيمَةً وَهَذَا كَنَ أَصَابَتْهُ مَخمَصَهُ مَا لَهُ شَلَوك : طَعَاير غيره رحمهُ لَا إِبَاحَةُ مُطَلقَةً حَتى أَذَاتَرَكَ مَاتَ كَان شهيدا عَلَا فِي طَعَامِرَ نَفْسِهِ وَإِذَا اعْتَوفا: ضَمن لكونِهِ وَمَن في نفسه ومالكَ مِثلُ تَنَاوُلِ تَلُو الأَحَرَامِ عَن ضُرُونَ بِالمخرم انه يرخص له ويضمن الجزاء فكن لله منياه والله اعلم . بالصّوابِ واليه المرجع والمآب
In the [ʻazima] a necessity does not bear the dispensation by the evidence. And the dispensation was entered in performance for the form. And because it preceded that the hope in legislation is tawhid and iman, and the origin in belief, and performance in it is a pillar referred to, so it became the pillars of the Sharia and the foundation of the religion that does not bear falling and transgressing from the lawgiver, exalted. And other than it became a temporary rule. And what is from the rights of servants... Another section: it bears falling by its origin but the guide of falling was not found, and what he desired is a matter above it, so the operation becomes necessary by establishing the dispensation. And what is necessary by its origin is made as its origin [ʻazima]. This is like one who is afflicted with famine, having no food, someone else's food? Not absolute permissibility, so if he leaves it and dies, he is a martyr. And if he takes it, he compensates because of his own life and property? Like eating hunted game? Out of necessity by the haram: he is permitted and compensates the price. So it is for Allah's decree. And Allah knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and abode.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a legal discussion about the relationship between 'azima (the original, stricter ruling) and rukhsah (dispensation) in the Sharia. It begins by stating that some necessities do not admit a dispensation because the original ruling is foundational. For example, the obligations of tawhid (monotheism) and iman (faith) are essential and cannot be dropped. Similarly, performance of prayer (adā') is a pillar that cannot be abandoned. These are the 'umad (pillars) of the Sharia and the foundation of the religion, which do not tolerate falling away or transgression. In contrast, other rulings are temporary and subject to change due to necessity. The passage then divides rulings into categories: some fall due to their nature from the rights of Allah, and others are from the rights of servants. It then introduces another category: a ruling that may fall by its origin but the evidence for falling is not present, so the original ruling remains and the dispensation is not applied. The example given is of a person in dire need (makhmasa) who has no food but finds the food of another person. The ruling is not absolute permissibility (ibāḥa muṭlaqa); rather, if he takes it to save his life, he is permitted but must compensate the owner (yaḍman al-qīma). If he refrains and dies, he is considered a martyr. This illustrates that dispensation does not abrogate the original rule entirely; the obligation to compensate remains. The same principle applies to eating from the haram (e.g., game in the sanctuary) under necessity: permission is granted with liability to pay the penalty (jazaʼ). Thus, the passage emphasizes that rukhsah is restricted and does not remove all obligations.

Key Terms
العزيمة (al-ʻazīma): The original, stricter ruling that applies under normal circumstances without concession.
الرخصة (al-rukḥṣa): A dispensation that permits a normally prohibited or relaxed act due to necessity or need.
الضرورة (al-ḍarūra): A necessity that permits what is normally prohibited, but only to the extent needed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of a person afflicted by famine who finds another's food, what is the ruling according to this passage?
A) He may eat it freely without any obligation.
B) He may eat it but must compensate the owner.
C) He must not eat it even if he dies.
D) He may eat it and is not liable because of necessity.

Key Takeaway
Rukhsah permits the prohibited act under necessity but retains the obligation to compensate or pay a penalty—dispensation does not abrogate all duties.

Definitions of Haqiqah and Majaz
Defining haqiqah and majaz with their linguistic roots and methodological analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-haqiqah and al-majaz, explain their modes of acquisition, and articulate the analogy between the haqiqah/majaz and nass/qiyas relationships.

Core Idea
Haqiqah is literal and permanent; majaz is metaphorical and derived, just as qiyas derives from nass.

Before we interpret any legal text, we must distinguish literal from metaphorical meaning. This passage from al-Bazdawi lays foundational definitions: haqiqah is the original, transmitted meaning; majaz is secondary, obtained through reflection. These categories mirror the relationship between nass (primary text) and qiyas (analogical reasoning). Understanding this sharpens your ability to identify figurative language in the Quran and Hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definitions
Read the passage and extract the exact definitions of haqiqah and majaz. Note the key phrases: haqiqah is 'what was coined for' and majaz is 'other than what it was coined for'.
The definition is the first sentence after 'وتفسير القسم الثالث'.
2
Trace the Linguistic Roots
Look at the derivations given: haqiqah from the root haqqa (truth), and majaz from jaza (to exceed/pass). Understand that these roots inform the conceptual meaning.
Haqiqah is related to 'truth' – the word 'fits' its meaning.
3
Compare Modes of Acquisition
Note the two different paths: haqiqah is attained only through sam'a (transmission) and never ceases. Majaz is attained through ta'ammul (reflection) and is compared to nass and qiyas.
Sam'a refers to revelation or established usage; ta'ammul requires reasoning.
4
Apply the Analogy
The passage explicitly compares the relationship of majaz to haqiqah with that of qiyas to nass. Reflect on why: just as qiyas derives from nass, majaz derives from haqiqah. Both are secondary and dependent.
This analogy is a key methodological principle in Hanafi usul.
5
Note the Permanence of Haqiqah
The text says haqiqah 'never ceases from the named item forever'. This means literal meaning always remains a possibility and must be considered first in interpretation.
This is a default rule: preference for haqiqah unless there is a valid clue for majaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفسير القسم الثالث: أن الحقيقة اسم لكل لفظ أُريد به ما وضع له مأخوذ من حَقَّ الشيء، يحق حقاً، فهو حق، وحاق، وحقيق، ومنه سميت : الحاقة.
والمجاز : اسم لما أُريد به غيرُ ما وضع له.
مفعل من جاز، يجوز، بمعنى فاعل، أي: متعد عن أصله.
ولا تُنال الحقيقة إلا بالسماع ولا تسقط عن المسمى أبداً.
والمجاز يُنال بالتأمل في طريقه، وذلك مثل النص والقياس؛ فيُعتبر به، ويُحتذى بمثاله.
ومثال المجاز من الحقيقة مثال القياس من النص.
And the interpretation of the third division: that al-Haqiqah is a name for every word intended for what it was coined for, derived from 'haqqa al-shay' (the thing became true), yahaqqu haqqan, so it is haqq, and haq, and haqiq; and from it is named al-Haqqah. And al-Majaz: a name for that which is intended for other than what it was coined for. It is the ism maf'ul from 'jaza, yajuzu', meaning 'fa'il' (active participle), i.e., that which departs from its origin. And al-Haqiqah is only attained through sam'a' (hearing/transmission) and never ceases from the named item forever. And al-Majaz is attained through reflection on its path, such as nass and qiyas; thus it is considered by it and its example is followed. And the example of majaz from haqiqah is like the example of qiyas from nass.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defines two fundamental categories of linguistic usage in usul al-fiqh: haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical). The author presents the derivation of the term haqiqah from the root meaning 'truth' or 'reality' (haqq), and points to the verse 'al-Haqqah' as a related Quranic term. Majaz is derived from 'jaza' meaning to pass or exceed, indicating that a word is used beyond its original meaning. Key points: (1) Haqiqah is acquired only through sam'a (transmission/revelation) and is permanent – it never ceases to apply to its named object. (2) Majaz is acquired through reflection (ta'ammul) on its path, which involves nass (text) and qiyas (analogy). This mirrors the relationship between nass (original ruling) and qiyas (derived ruling). Thus, just as qiyas depends on nass, majaz depends on haqiqah. The passage establishes the methodological principle that metaphorical meanings are secondary and require evidence from literal usage or analogical reasoning.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): A word used for what it was originally coined for; it is attained only through sam'a (transmission) and cannot be detached from its original referent.
المجاز (al-majāz): A word used for other than its original meaning; attained through reflection (ta'ammul) and analogy (qiyas), and it is compared to qiyas in relation to nass.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is haqiqah attained according to the passage?
A) Through qiyas
B) Through sam'a (transmission)
C) Through reflection (ta'ammul)
D) Through consensus

Key Takeaway
Haqiqah is the foundation, attained only by transmission; majaz is a secondary meaning derived via reflection, never detached from its literal root.

Istidlal bi Ibarah and Isharah
Defining istidlal bi ibarah and isharah with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between istidlal bi ibarah and istidlal bi isharah and identify each in a Quranic text.

Core Idea
Ibarah derives the direct ruling; Isharah extracts an implied ruling linguistically valid but not primary.

In Hanafi usul, not all rulings are directly stated. Some are implied by the text's structure. This lesson introduces two key inference methods: ibarah (expression) – the apparent meaning for which the text was revealed, and isharah (indication) – a secondary meaning derived linguistically. Mastering these tools is essential for accurate istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Define istidlal bi ibarah
Ibarah is acting upon the literal and intended meaning of the text – the primary ruling the text was brought for.
Example: 'Establish prayer' directly commands prayer.
2
Define istidlal bi isharah
Isharah is acting upon a meaning established by the text's wording but not its primary intention – an implied ruling.
The text is like a man looking at one thing while perceiving another by his glances.
3
Apply to the Quranic example
In Surah al-Hashr (59:8), the verse states that spoils go to the poor emigrants. The ibarah is the entitlement to spoils. The description 'poor' indicates (isharah) that they lost their property in Makkah to the enemy.
This implied ruling about loss of ownership is derived via isharah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفسير القسم الرابع : أن الاستدلال بعبارة النص هو : العمل بظاهر ما سيق الكلام له. والاستدلال بإشارته هو : العمل بما ثبت بنظمه لغةً، لكنه غير مقصود، ولا سيق له النص، وليس بظاهر من كل وجه، فسميناه : إشارة. كرجل ينظر ببصره إلى شيء، ويُدرك مع ذلك غيره بإشارة لحظاته. ونظيره : قوله تعالى: ﴿ لِلْفُقَرَاءِ الْمُهَجِرِينَ الَّذِينَ أُخْرِجُوا مِن دِيَارِهِمْ وَأَمْوَالِهِمْ الحشر / ۸ ، وإنما سيق النص لاستحقاق سهم من الغنيمة على سبيل الترجمة لما سبق من أول الآية، واسمُ الفقراء إشارة إلى زوال ملكهم عما خلَّفوا في دار الحرب لاستيلاء الكفار. وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَعَلَى الْمَوْلُودِ لَهُ رِزْقُهُنَّ وَكِسْوَتُهُنَّ البقرة / ۲۳۳ : سيق
And the interpretation of the fourth division: that istidlal (inference) by the expression (ibarah) of the text is: acting upon the apparent meaning for which the speech was intended. And inference by its indication (isharah) is: acting upon what is established by its construction linguistically, but it is not intended, nor was the text brought for it, and it is not apparent from every aspect, so we call it indication. Like a man looking with his eye at something, and he perceives besides it something else by the indication of his glances. And its analogy: the saying of Allah Almighty: 'For the poor emigrants who were driven from their homes and their properties' [Al-Hashr 8], and the text was only brought for entitlement to a share of the spoils as a description for what preceded from the beginning of the verse, and the name 'the poor' is an indication of the loss of their ownership of what they left behind in the land of war due to the domination of the disbelievers. And the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And upon the father of the child is their provision and clothing' [Al-Baqarah 233]: it was brought...
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage introduces two methods of extracting rulings from the Quran: istidlal bi ibarah (inference by expression) and istidlal bi isharah (inference by indication). Ibarah refers to the direct, apparent meaning of the text for which it was revealed. Isharah refers to a secondary meaning that is linguistically valid but not the primary intention of the text. The author compares isharah to a man who looks at one thing but also perceives another by his glances. The example from Surah al-Hashr: the verse states that spoils go to the poor emigrants, which is the ibarah (direct meaning). However, the description 'the poor' also indicates (isharah) that they have lost ownership of their property in Dar al-Harb due to the enemy's control. This implied ruling about loss of ownership is isharah. The second example from Surah al-Baqarah is incomplete but shows that the text's primary purpose is about provision, but may also indicate other rulings.

Key Terms
الاستدلال بعبارة النص (al-istidlal bi ibarah al-nass): Inference based on the literal and intended meaning of the text.
الاستدلال بإشارة النص (al-istidlal bi isharat al-nass): Inference based on an implied meaning that is linguistically valid but not the main intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes istidlal bi isharah from istidlal bi ibarah?
A) Isharah applies only to the Sunnah, ibarah to the Quran
B) Isharah derives a meaning that is linguistically valid but not the primary intent of the text
C) Isharah is used when ibarah is ambiguous
D) Isharah is a weaker form of qiyas

Key Takeaway
Istidlal bi ibarah and isharah are complementary tools: ibarah gives the direct ruling, isharah gives implied rulings that are linguistically sound.

Usul Principle: Prohibition as Metaphor for Negation
Explains why fasting at night is invalid and prohibition means negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi usul treats the prohibition (nahy) of continuous fasting as a negation (nafy) and apply this rule to identify when nahy is metaphorical.

Core Idea
Prohibition (nahy) in Shari'ah can be metaphorical for negation (nafy) when the act is impossible.

In this passage from al-Pazdawi, a key usul principle is illustrated: the word 'prohibition' (nahy) is sometimes used metaphorically to mean 'negation' (nafy). The case is continuous fasting (wisal), which is not legislated. Understanding when nahy implies nafy is crucial for correct usul reasoning and avoiding over-application of haram rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
Recognize that 'continuous fasting' (wisal) involves fasting day and night without break, including night hours.
This is the act under study.
2
Recognize the apparent prohibition
The hadith says 'Do not practice continuous fasting' (لا تواصلوا). This is an apparent prohibition (nahy).
The word 'nahy' is used.
3
Analyze why the prohibition is interpreted as negation
The author argues that continuous fasting is not legislated in Shari'ah and is impossible to perform as a valid act of worship. Therefore, the prohibition cannot be literal; it must be metaphorical negation, meaning the act is invalid.
Impossibility is the key condition.
4
Extract the usul rule
When a prohibited act is impossible to perform validly, the prohibition (nahy) is reinterpreted as negation (nafy) of its validity, not as a declaration of haram.
This is a Hanafi usul principle.
5
Apply the rule to a similar case
For instance, fasting on the two Eids is prohibited; the prohibition is also understood as negation (invalidity) because those days are not designated for fasting.
Parallel application reinforces the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك صوم الليالي؛ لأن الوصال غير مشروع، ولا ممكن، والنهار المتعين لشهوة البطن غالبًا، فتعين للصوم؛ تحقيقًا للابتلاء، فصار النهي مستعارًا عن النفي.
Similarly, fasting at night [is not valid]; because continuous fasting (wisal) is not legislated and is not possible, and the day is specifically [the time] for the appetite of the stomach generally, so [the day] became designated for fasting, to actualize the trial, thus the prohibition (nahy) became borrowed for negation.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on fasting at night. The author states that fasting at night is not valid because the fast of wisal (continuous fasting day and night) is not legislated in the Shari'ah and is not possible. The day is naturally the time for eating to satisfy hunger, so the fast is assigned to the daytime to achieve the purpose of trial and worship. The word 'prohibition' in the hadith against continuous fasting is actually used in the sense of 'negation' (that the act is not established, not that it is prohibited in the sense of haram). This is a usul principle: sometimes nahy is used metaphorically for nafy (negation).

Key Terms
الوصال (al-wisal): continuous fasting without break at night
النهي (al-nahy): prohibition; here used metaphorically to mean negation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the prohibition of continuous fasting (wisal) considered a metaphor for negation (nafy) rather than a literal prohibition?
A) Because continuous fasting is impossible to perform as a valid worship.
B) Because the day is specifically for appetite and night is not.
C) Because the hadith uses the word 'nahy' but the act is not possible to perform.
D) Because fasting at night is not legislated.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a prohibition (nahy) may be metaphorical negation (nafy) when the prohibited act is impossible, as in the case of continuous fasting.

The Legal Effect of Muslim Domination by Harbis: 'Isma and Its Cause
Explains causation of 'isma and its effect on prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the prohibition against taking life and property falls for a Muslim dominated by harbis and identify the cause of 'isma as secure possession in Dar al-Islam.

Core Idea
Protection (isma) is limited by its cause—secure possession in Islamic territory—so when that is lost, the prohibition lapses in worldly rulings.

When a Muslim falls under the control of harbis (enemy non-Muslims), an important legal shift occurs. The prohibition against violating his life and property is not absolute; it depends on the continuity of 'isma, which is tied to living in Dar al-Islam. Our source passage from Usul al-Bazdawi explains this principle using causation (ta'lil).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine what ruling is being discussed: the prohibition (nahy) against harbis dominating Muslims.
2
Understand the key concept: 'isma
Define 'isma as the inviolability of life and property that protects a Muslim from harm.
'Isma is the foundation for the prohibition.
3
Recognize the cause (sabab) of 'isma
Identify that 'isma arises from secure possession by abode (ihraz bi al-dar) — living in Dar al-Islam.
This cause is what establishes protection.
4
Apply the principle of causality
When the cause ends (Muslim is taken by harbis, breaking the link to Dar al-Islam), the effect ('isma) also ends in worldly matters.
The prohibition is contingent on 'isma.
5
Distinguish between worldly and hereafter rulings
While the worldly ruling (hukm al-dunya) may change, the sin remains if harm is done, because the original prohibition had a higher basis.
The passage says 'in hukm al-dunya'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما استيلاء أهل الحرب، فإنما صار منهياً بواسطة العصمة، ثابتة في حقنا، دون أهل الحرب؛ لانقطاع ولايتنا عنهم، ولأن العصمة متناهية بتناهي سببها، وهو الإحرازُ بالدار، فسقط النهي في حكم الدنيا.
As for the domination of the people of war [over Muslims], it is prohibited only by virtue of protection (isma) established in our right, not in the right of the people of war, due to the discontinuity of our authority over them, and because protection is limited by the limitation of its cause, which is secure possession by abode, so the prohibition falls in the worldly ruling.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of a Muslim falling under the control of harbis (enemy non-Muslims). Such domination is prohibited for Muslims, but the prohibition is tied to the concept of isma (inviolability of life and property). Isma is established for Muslims by virtue of living in the Dar al-Islam. When a Muslim is taken by harbis, the authority (wilaya) of Muslims over that person is broken, and the cause of isma (which is secure possession in the Islamic abode) ends. Therefore, in worldly matters, the prohibition (against killing, etc.) may not apply as strictly, though it remains a sin.

Key Terms
العصمة (al-'isma): protection of life and property under Islamic law
الإحراز بالدار (al-ihraz bi al-dar): secure possession by virtue of living in Islamic territory

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the prohibition fall in worldly rulings when a Muslim is dominated by harbis?
A) Because harbis are not bound by Islamic law.
B) Because the cause of 'isma (secure possession by abode) ends.
C) Because the Muslim loses his faith.
D) Because the prohibition is only for the Hereafter.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition against harming a Muslim is contingent on 'isma, which depends on secure possession in Dar al-Islam; when that cause is lost, the worldly ruling changes.

Zina and Affinity Prohibitions: The Causal Chain in Hanafi Usul
States that zina itself does not establish marriage prohibitions by affinity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the causal chain (sabab–musabbab) in the context of hurmat al-musaharah and apply the Hanafi principle that zina does not directly create marriage prohibitions.

Core Idea
Zina does not create affinity prohibitions; only the resulting child does.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul clarifies a crucial distinction: the act of zina is merely a physical cause (sabab) for the fluid, which itself is a cause for the child. The child is the true basis for marriage prohibitions by affinity. Understanding this causal chain prevents conflating the sin with its natural consequences in legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on zina's effect
First, note the explicit ruling: 'As for zina, it does not itself cause the prohibition of affinity at all.' This is the core statement.
The phrase 'بِنَفْسِهِ' (by itself) is key: zina is not a direct cause.
2
Trace the causal chain
Break down the sequence: zina → fluid (sperm) → child → prohibition. The author describes zina as a cause (sabab) for the fluid, and the fluid as a cause for the child's existence.
Each step is a sabab for the next; only the child is the direct origin (asl) of the prohibition.
3
Apply the principle of direct causation
In Hanafi usul, the legal effect (hukm) attaches to the immediate cause, not to remote antecedents. Here, the prohibition of affinity is attached to the child (the immediate cause), not to zina.
This principle is rooted in the maxim: 'التابع تابع' (the follower follows the origin).
4
Derive the ruling for a scenario
Consider a man who commits zina with a woman. According to this chain, he may marry the woman's mother (no affinity prohibition) because no valid marriage occurred. However, he must not marry his own biological daughter (blood prohibition) because the child is the origin.
Distinguish between blood prohibitions (mahram by lineage) and affinity prohibitions (mahram by marriage).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الزنا: فلا يوجب حرمة المصاهرة أصلاً بنفسه، وإنما هو سبب للماء، والماء سبب للولد وجوداً والولد هو الأصل في استحقاق الحرمات، ولا عصيان ولا عدوان فيه.
As for zina, it does not itself cause the prohibition of affinity at all; rather, it is a cause for the fluid, and the fluid is a cause for the child's existence, and the child is the origin in the entitlement of prohibitions, and there is no disobedience nor aggression in it.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the act of zina (illicit sexual intercourse) does not itself create marriage prohibitions (haram al-musahara) that would prevent marriage between relatives. Zina is only a cause (sabab) for the emission of fluid (water), and that fluid is a cause for the existence of a child. It is the child that is the basis for establishing prohibitions (like the child's lineage). The act of zina itself does not involve obedience or aggression in the context of these prohibitions; it is merely a physical cause.

Key Terms
حرمة المصاهرة (hurmat al-musaharah): prohibitions of marriage by affinity (e.g., marrying the mother of one's wife)
الماء (al-ma'): sperm, the fluid causing conception

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the role of zina regarding marriage prohibitions?
A) It directly creates prohibition of affinity.
B) It is a cause for the fluid, which can lead to a child who creates prohibitions.
C) It is a sin that nullifies all marriage ties.
D) It establishes the child's lineage automatically.

Key Takeaway
Zina does not create affinity prohibitions; only the child resulting from that union can create blood prohibitions.

Metaphor in Legal Language: Talaq for Itq Dispute
Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on isti'ara of talaq for itq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the validity of using 'talaq' metaphorically for 'itq' and apply the Hanafi condition of specific shared meaning in legal metaphor.

Core Idea
Metaphor in legal language requires a specific shared meaning; general similarity is insufficient.

This passage addresses a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on whether the word 'talaq' (divorce) can be used metaphorically for 'itq' (emancipation). The Hanafis argue that metaphorical usage requires a specific, defining shared meaning, not just broad similarities. We will analyze both positions and understand the Hanafi emphasis on precision in legal language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i argument
Extract Imam al-Shafi'i's claim that talaq can be used metaphorically for itq because both are annulments built on pervasiveness and bindingness, and general appropriateness in meaning suffices for metaphor.
Note the Shafi'i reliance on 'munasaba' (appropriateness) in meanings.
2
State the Hanafi response
The Hanafis reject this, stating that a metaphor requires a specific shared meaning (mushakala) that defines the original term, not any general similarity.
They compare the Shafi'i position to an invalid method of causation (ta'lil) by any attribute without specific effect.
3
Examine the Hanafi linguistic analogy
The Hanafis cite the Arab usage of 'lion' for a brave man: the metaphor works only due to the specific attribute of bravery, not any random attribute.
This illustrates the requirement for a specific shared characteristic.
4
Analyze the Hanafi definitions of talaq and itq
Talaq means 'removal of a restriction', while itq means 'establishment of legal capacity'. These are fundamentally different; one removes, the other establishes.
The Hanafis further explain the linguistic root of itq from 'becoming strong' (like a bird flying).
5
Consider the secondary case of sale and lease
The text addresses an objection: using 'sale' for 'lease' may be valid in certain loci (free person's labor) but invalid for usufructs, showing that invalidity may stem from the locus, not the metaphor itself.
This reinforces that the metaphor's validity depends on the suitability of the locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : أن يُستعار الطلاق للعتق؛ لأنهما يصح يتشابهان في المعاني ؛ لأن كل واحد منهما إسقاط بُني على السراية، واللزوم والمناسبة في المعاني من أسباب الاستعارة، مثل المناسبة في الأسباب. وقلنا : لا تصح هذه الاستعارة؛ لما قلنا في المسألة الأولى: إن اتصال الفرع بالأصل في حق الأصل: في حكم العدم. ولا تصح الاستعارة للمناسبة في المعاني من الوجه الذي قلنا؛ لأن طريق الاستعارة من قبل المعاني: المشاكلةُ في المعاني، التي هي من قبيل الاختصاص الذي به يقوم الموجود، فأما بكل معنى : فلا . وهذا الطريق من الخصم نظير طريقه في أوصاف النص، أن التعليل بكل وصف صحيح من غير أثر خاص. وقلنا نحن : هو باطل؛ لأن الابتلاء يسقط، فكذلك الاستعارة تقع بمعنى له أثر الاختصاص. ألا يُرى أن العرب تسمي الشجاع أسداً؛ للاشتراك في المعنى الخاص، وهو الشجاعة، فأما بكل وصف فلا؛ لأن ذلك يُبطل الامتحان، وتصير الموجوداتُ في الأحكام كلّها متناسبةً ولا مناسبة هذا الوجه ؛ لأن معنى الطلاق ما وضع له اسمه، وما احتمله محله ، وهو رفع القيد ؛ لأن الإطلاق عبارة عنه، والنكاح لا يوجب حقيقة الرق، ولا يَسلُّب المالكية، وإنما يوجب قيداً: فلا يحتمل إلا إطلاق القيد. وأما الإعتاق : فإثبات القوة الشرعية ؛ لأن ذلك معناه لغةً. يقال : عَتَقَ الطير: إذا قوي وطار عن وكره، ومنه: عتاق الطير، ويقال : عَتَقَت البكر : إذا أدركت، وهذا شائع في كلام العرب. وكذلك الرق ثابت على الكمال، وسلطان المالكية ساقط، فصح الإعتاق إثباتاً. وليس بين إزالة القيد لتعمل القوة الشرعية عملها، وبين إثباتها بعد العدم مشابهة، كما ليس بين إحياء الميت وبين إطلاق الحي مشابهة، فما هذا إلا كمَن استعار الحمار للذكي، والأسد للجبان. فإن قيل: أليس لا يصح أن يُستعار البيع للإجارة، كما لا تُستعار الإجارة للبيع، وملك المنفعة تابع لملك الرقبة. قيل له : قد قال بعض مشايخنا رحمهم الله : إن البيع لا ينعقد بلفظ الإجارة، والإجارة تنعقد به وذلك يُتصوّر فى الحر، تقول: بعتُ نفسي منك شهراً بدرهم العمل كذا، وهذا جائز. فأما إذا قال: بعث منك منافع هذه الدارِ شهراً بكذا: لم يجز. كذا ذكره في أول كتاب الصلح. وهذا ليس لفساد في الاستعارة لكن لفساد في المحل ؛ لأن المنفعة لا تصلح محلاً للإضافة ؛ لأن ذلك معدوم ليس في مقدور البشر. حتى لو أضاف إليها الإجارة : لم يجز، فكذلك ما يُستعار لها، ولكن العينَ أُقيمت مقامها في حق الإضافة في الأصل، فكذلك فيما يُستعار لها، وصار هذا كالبيع يُستعار للنكاح في غير محله وهي المَحرَم من النساء، فثبت أن فساده لإضافته إلى غير محله.
Al-Shafi'i (rahimahullah) said: That talaq (divorce) can be used metaphorically for itq (emancipation); because they are similar in meanings, since each of them is an annulment based on pervasiveness and bindingness, and appropriateness in meanings is a cause for metaphor, like appropriateness in causes. We (Hanafis) said: This metaphor is not valid; for what we said in the first issue: That the connection of the branch to the root with respect to the root is in the state of non-existence. And the metaphor is not valid for appropriateness in meanings from the aspect we mentioned; because the method of metaphor from the perspective of meanings is similarity in meanings, which is of the type of specificity by which an existent is established; as for every meaning, no. And this method of the opponent is analogous to his method in attributes of the text: that causation (ta'lil) by any attribute is valid without specific effect. And we said: It is false; because testing (k) falls away; thus metaphor occurs with a meaning having the effect of specificity. Do you not see that the Arabs call the brave a lion, for the shared specific meaning - bravery; as for every attribute, no; because that invalidates examination and all existents would become proportionate in rulings, and there is no proportion in this manner. For the meaning of talaq is what its name is assigned to, and what its locus can bear, which is removal of a restriction; because 'release' (al-iṭlāq) is an expression for it, and marriage does not necessitate true slavery nor does it negate ownership, but rather imposes a restriction: so it bears only removal of the restriction. As for itq (emancipation), it is the establishment of legal capacity (al-quwwa al-sharʿiyya), because that is its meaning linguistically. It is said: ʿataqa al-ṭayr (the bird was set free) when it became strong and flew from its nest, hence 'ʿitāq al-ṭayr' (noble birds), and ʿataqat al-bikr (the girl attained maturity) when she came of age, and this is common in Arab speech. Similarly, slavery is complete in its presence, and the authority of ownership is absent, so emancipation is valid as establishment. There is no similarity between removing a restriction to allow legal capacity to operate and establishing it after non-existence, just as there is no similarity between reviving the dead and releasing the living. This is only like one who metaphorically calls a clever person a donkey, or a coward a lion. If it is said: Is it not invalid to use the word 'sale' metaphorically for lease, just as lease is not used metaphorically for sale, and ownership of usufruct is subsidiary to ownership of the corpus? It is said to him: Some of our shaykhs (rahimahumullah) have stated: That a sale is not concluded with the word 'lease', but a lease is concluded with it, and that is conceivable for a free person: you say, 'I sold myself to you for a month for a dirham to do such-and-such work,' and that is permissible. But if he says, 'I sold to you the usufructs of this house for a month for such-and-such,' it is not permissible. Thus it is mentioned in the beginning of the Book of Sulh (Compromise). And this is not due to the invalidity of the metaphor but due to the invalidity of the locus because usufruct is not a valid locus for attribution; since it is non-existent, not within human capacity. Even if one attributes lease to it, it is not permissible, so too with what is used metaphorically for it. But the object (al-ʿayn) is substituted in its place for attribution in the original, so likewise for what is metaphorically used for it. And this is like sale being used metaphorically for marriage in its invalid locus, which is a mahram woman. So it is established that its invalidity is due to attributing it to an improper locus.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the permissibility of using the word 'talaq' (divorce) metaphorically for 'itq' (emancipation of a slave). The Shafi'i argument is that both acts share common meanings such as 'annulment' and 'pervasiveness' (fitting that they both sever ties), and appropriateness in meaning is a valid basis for metaphorical usage. The Hanafi response, however, refutes this. First, they reiterate a principle from earlier: a branch (far') connected to its root (asl) is, in respect to the root, considered non-existent; thus a metaphorical link cannot be established based on a mere general similarity. Second, the Hanafis argue that true isti'ara (metaphor) in legal language requires a specific shared attribute that is essential to the meaning of the original term. They illustrate this with the Arabic example: a brave man is metaphorically called a 'lion' due to the specific attribute of 'bravery', not any random attribute. Similarly, the specific meaning of talaq is 'removal of a restriction' (reflecting the linguistic sense of 'release'), while the specific meaning of itq is 'establishment of legal capacity' (derived from the root meaning 'strength/freedom'). For itq, since the slave already possesses complete legal capacity in potential (because ownership is not absolute), emancipation 'establishes' that capacity. Talaq, on the other hand, only removes a marital tie. The Hanafis conclude that there is no genuine similarity between these two concepts; they compare the attempted metaphor to calling a clever person a 'donkey' or a coward a 'lion'—outright false. The text then addresses a secondary objection about using the word 'sale' (bay') for 'lease' (ijara). Some Hanafi scholars note that while you cannot use 'lease' to mean 'sale', you can use 'sale' to mean 'lease' when the object is a free person's labor (because the person is a 'locus' capable of being sold), but not when it concerns usufructs of property (since the usufruct is not a valid locus for direct attribution). This demonstrates that the invalidity is not due to the metaphor itself but due to the unsuitability of the locus. Overall, the Hanafi position stresses that metaphorical language in legal contexts must be grounded in precise, specific shared meanings—not vague generalities—to avoid undermining the integrity of legal tests (imtiḥān) and the clarity of legal categories.

Key Terms
الاستعارة (al-istiʿāra): Metaphorical usage of a word from its original meaning to another due to a shared specific attribute.
الابتلاء (al-ibtilāʾ): The test or standard by which legal language is measured; applying metaphor with any vague similarity would undermine this test.
القوة الشرعية (al-quwwa al-sharʿiyya): Legal capacity (or freedom) that is established by emancipation; analogous to a bird gaining strength to fly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is it invalid to use the word 'talaq' metaphorically for 'itq'?
A) Because both acts are completely unrelated in meaning.
B) Because the specific meaning of talaq (removal of a restriction) is fundamentally different from the specific meaning of itq (establishment of legal capacity).
C) Because the Shafi'i school does not allow such metaphors.
D) Because marriage is not a form of slavery.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, valid legal metaphor (isti'ara) requires a specific shared meaning that is essential to the original term, not mere general similarity.

The Rule of Haqiqah over Majaz: Linguistic Foundations
Establishing the rule of haqiqah over majaz with linguistic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that haqiqah takes precedence over majaz when possible by analyzing the linguistic examples of aqra', aqd, and nikah.

Core Idea
Literal meaning always beats metaphorical in Hanafi usul.

This lesson explores the foundational usul principle that when a word has both a literal and a metaphorical meaning, the literal must be used if possible in context. We will examine key linguistic examples from the Hanafi school to understand how this rule operates and why it is essential for correct legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Identify the principle: العمل بالحقيقة متى أمكن سقط المجاز (acting upon the literal whenever possible abrogates the metaphorical). This is a default rule in Hanafi usul.
The rule is a general maxim, not absolute – it applies unless the literal is impossible or abandoned.
2
Understand the linguistic basis of haqiqah and majaz
Recognize that words have primary (haqiqah) meanings coined in language, and secondary (majaz) meanings transferred due to relationship or adjacency. The literal meaning is the origin; metaphor borrows from it.
Haqlqah is the original coinage; majaz is a transfer from that origin.
3
Analyze the example of aqra'
Trace the derivation of aqra' from the root word meaning 'gathering' (of blood) and 'transition'. Both point to menstruation as the literal meaning, while purity is metaphorical due to adjacency (the period of purity between menses).
The Hanafi school adopts haqiqah: aqra' = menstruation, leading to legal rulings on 'iddah.
4
Apply to aqd and nikah
Show how aqd literally means 'tying' (e.g., a knot), but is used metaphorically for a contract or resolution. Similarly, nikah literally means 'union' or 'intercourse', while the marriage contract is a metaphorical extension because it is a cause of that union.
These examples demonstrate that the literal meaning must be used unless the metaphor is forced by impossibility.
5
Recognize the implication for legal reasoning
The rule means that in interpreting texts, the literal meaning is the default. If the literal is possible, any metaphorical interpretation is invalid. This guides Hanafi jurists in deriving rulings from Quran and Sunnah.
This principle is a cornerstone of Hanafi literalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإيجاب ما زاد على الثلاث من طريق الحكم باطل، لكن من طريق التكلم صحيح، والاستثناء تصرُّفٌ في التكلم بالمنع ، فصح، فكذا هذا، : لما كان تصرُّفاً في حق التكلم صحت الاستعارة به؛ لحكم حقيقته وإن لم ينعقد لإيجاب تلك الحقيقة، ومن حكم الحقيقة : عتقه من حين فجعل إقراراً به، فعَتَقَ في القضاء. بخلاف النداء؛ لأنه لاستحضار المنادَى بصورة الاسم، لا بمعناه، فإذا لم يكن المعنى مطلوباً : لم تجب الاستعارة لتصحيح معناه. بخلاف قوله : يا حر فإنه يستوي فيه نداؤه وخبره؛ لأنه موضوع للتحرير، فصار عينه قائماً مقام معناه، فصار المعنى مطلوباً بكل حال. ومن حكم هذا الباب أن العمل بالحقيقة متى أمكن سقط المجاز؛ لأن المستعار لا يزاحِمُ الأصل. وذلك مثل قولنا في الأقراء : إنها الحيض؛ لأن القُرْء للحيض حقيقة وللطهر مجاز؛ من قبل أنه مأخوذ من الجَمْع، وهو معنى حقيقة هذه العبارة لغةً، وذلك صفة الدم المجتمع. فأما الطهر: فإنما وصف به مجازاً بمجاورته، ولأن معنى القُرء: الانتقالُ، يقال: قرأ النجم: إذا انتقل والانتقال : بالحيض، لا بالطهر، فصارت الحقيقة أولى. وكذلك العقدُ لما ينعقد حقيقةٌ، وللعزم: مجاز، وكذلك النكاح: للجمع في لغة العرب على ما عُرف والاجتماعُ في الوطء، ويسمى العقد نكاحاً مجازاً؛ لأنه سببه ، حتى سُمِّي الوطء جماعاً، فكانت الحقيقة أولى. وأمثلة هذا أكثر من أن تُحصى. ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في الدعوى، في رجل له أمةٌ ولدت ثلاثة أولاد في بطون مختلفة، فقال المولى أحد هؤلاء ولدي، ثم مات قبل البيان: إنه يعتق من كل واحد ثلثه. ولا يُعتبر ما يصيبُ كلَّ واحدٍ منهم من قبل أمه، حتى يعتق الثالث كلّه، ونصفُ الثاني، كما قال أبو يوسف رحمه الله؛ لأن إصابته من قبل أمه في مقابلة إصابته من قِبَل نفسه بمنزلة المجاز من الحقيقة. وأمثلة هذا أكثر من أن تُحصى. وإذا كانت الحقيقة متعذرة أو مهجورة: صير إلى المجاز، بالإجماع؛ لعدم المزاحمة. أما المتعذِّر: فمثلُ الرجل يحلف لا يأكل من هذه النخلة، أو: من هذه الكرمة، أو القدر أنه يقع على ما يُتَّخذُ منه مجازاً. بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذه الشاة، أو: هذا الرطب : فإنه يقع على عينه ؛ لأن الحقيقة قائمة. وكذلك إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذا الدقيق: وقع على ما يتخذ منه؛ لأن الحقيقة متعذرة. وكذلك لو حلف لا يشرب من هذه البئر: لا يقع على الكرع، وهو حقيقة؛ لما قلنا. واختلفوا فيما إذا أكل عين الدقيق، أو تكلف، فكرَعَ من البئر.
And rendering binding more than three [divorces] is invalid in terms of legal effect, but valid in terms of speech. The exception is a disposal in speech by prohibiting, so it is valid, and so is this: because it was a disposal in the right of speech, the metaphor was valid; for the ruling of its reality, even if it does not take effect for the binding of that reality. And from the rulings of reality: his emancipation from the moment he made it an acknowledgment, so he is freed judicially. Contrary to the call [to someone], because it is to summon the called person by the form of the name, not by its meaning; so if the meaning is not sought, the metaphor is not necessary to correct its meaning. Contrary to his saying: O free one, for it is equal in call and narration because it is coined for emancipation, so its essence stands in place of its meaning, so the meaning is sought in any case. And from the rulings of this chapter: that acting upon the literal meaning whenever possible abrogates the metaphorical; because the borrowed does not compete with the original. That is like our saying regarding aqra': that it is menstruation; because aqra' is literal for menstruation and metaphorical for purity; because it is derived from gathering, which is the literal meaning of this expression in language, and that is the description of the collected blood. As for purity: it is described by it metaphorically due to its adjacency, and because the meaning of aqra' is transition; it is said: the star set when it moved, and transition is by menstruation, not by purity, so the literal meaning takes precedence. Likewise, aqd is literal for what is tied, and metaphorical for resolution; likewise, nikah: for union in the Arabic language as known, and the union in intercourse, and the contract is called nikah metaphorically because it is its cause, until intercourse is called jima', so the literal meaning takes precedence. Examples of this are more than can be counted. And for this reason Abu Hanifa said in claims, regarding a man who has a slave girl who gave birth to three children in different pregnancies, and the master said: one of these is my son, then died before clarification: that one third of each is freed. And what pertains to each of them from the mother is not considered, according to what Abu Yusuf said; because his relation from the mother in comparison to his relation from himself is like the metaphorical from the literal. Examples of this are more than can be counted. And when the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, then recourse is to the metaphorical, by consensus, due to the lack of competition. As for the impossible: such as a man swears not to eat from this palm tree, or from this grapevine, or from this pot, that it falls on what is made from it metaphorically. Contrary to if he swears not to eat from this sheep, or this fresh date: it falls on its very self, because the literal meaning exists. Likewise, if he swears not to eat from this flour: it falls on what is made from it, because the literal meaning is impossible. Likewise, if he swears not to drink from this well: it does not fall on lapping [directly], which is literal, for what we said. And they differed regarding if one eats the flour itself, or struggles and laps from the well.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul (or its commentary) expounds the foundational usul rule: when the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word is possible in context, the metaphorical meaning (majaz) is invalid and must be abandoned. The author first illustrates this through linguistic examples: the word 'aqra' literally means menstruation (because it indicates collection of blood and transition), while its use for purity is only metaphorical due to adjacency. Similarly, 'aqd' literally means something tied, and 'nikah' literally means union (intercourse), so calling the marriage contract 'nikah' is metaphorical because it is a cause of union. The Hanafi madhhab applies this rule rigorously in fiqh, as shown in the example from Abu Hanifa: a man claims one of his slave girl's three children is his, then dies without specifying. Abu Hanifa rules that each child is freed from one-third of the estate, disregarding the mother's share (which would reduce some shares) because the literal meaning of 'one of these is my son' treats the claim equally, while considering the mother's share (as Abu Yusuf would) is metaphorical reasoning that must yield when literal possible. The passage then treats cases where literal meaning is impossible (e.g., eating a palm tree means eating dates made from it, not the wood; eating flour means eating bread; drinking from a well means water, not the act of lapping) or abandoned by custom (though not illustrated here). In such cases, by consensus, metaphor is used because the literal is unavailable. The final sentence notes a disagreement among scholars about whether eating the actual flour or lapping from the well violates the oath. This reinforces that the rule is default but subject to ikhtilaf when literal act is physically possible but impractical.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as coined in language.
المجاز (al-majaz): The metaphorical, transferred meaning of a word used due to a relationship (like cause-effect) or adjacency.
الأقراء (al-aqra'): The term used in Quran for waiting periods of women; here argued to literally mean menstruation, not purity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul rule discussed in this passage, what is the status of the metaphorical meaning (majaz) when the literal meaning (haqiqah) is possible in context?
A) The metaphorical meaning is also allowed to accommodate different interpretations.
B) The metaphorical meaning is invalid and must be abandoned.
C) Both meanings are considered equally, and the judge chooses.
D) The metaphorical meaning takes precedence because it is more common.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul rule mandates that the literal meaning of a word is the default in legal interpretation, and the metaphorical meaning is only used when the literal is impossible or abandoned.

Application of the Rule in Inheritance: The Slave Girl's Children
Applying the haqiqah-precedence rule to a practical fiqh case of paternity claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that haqiqah takes precedence over majaz to a complex fiqh scenario, tracing how Abu Hanifa used this rule to decide a case of emancipation and inheritance.

Core Idea
In legal claims, the literal wording is prioritized over secondary considerations.

This lesson applies the usul rule of haqiqah over majaz to a concrete fiqh scenario: a man claims one of his slave girl's three children is his son, then dies without specifying. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ on how to distribute emancipation. We will see how Abu Hanifa's ruling embodies the principle that literal meaning must be upheld.

Guided Path
1
Describe the scenario
A man owns a slave girl who gives birth to three children from different pregnancies. The man says, 'One of these is my son,' then dies without specifying which child. The children are now claiming freedom and inheritance.
The key is that the man's statement is a claim of paternity, not a will.
2
Identify the dispute
Abu Hanifa rules that each child is freed from one-third of the master's estate, without considering the mother's share. Abu Yusuf considers the mother's share, reducing the shares of some children. Abu Hanifa's view is based on the rule of haqiqah.
The mother's share here refers to the fact that the mother is a slave, so the child's paternity claim also involves the mother's status.
3
Apply the rule of haqiqah
The master's statement 'one of these is my son' is literal: it identifies the child as his son, implying full freedom and inheritance. The literal meaning treats all three equally because no specification is made. Abu Yusuf's consideration of the mother's share is seen as a metaphorical interference that should not override the literal claim.
The text says: 'لأن إصابته من قبل أمه في مقابلة إصابته من قِبَل نفسه بمنزلة المجاز من الحقيقة' (his relation from the mother compared to his relation from himself is like majaz compared to haqiqah).
4
Derive the legal outcome
Since the literal meaning is possible (the claim is clear), any metaphorical reasoning (like considering mother's share) must yield. So each child is freed from one-third of the estate, and no further adjustment is made.
This leads to equal shares of emancipation for the three children.
5
Recognize the broader principle
This case exemplifies how the usul rule directly impacts furu' fiqh rulings. Abu Hanifa's decision is not arbitrary but based on a consistent linguistic methodology.
The passage shows that usul rules are not abstract but have practical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإيجاب ما زاد على الثلاث من طريق الحكم باطل، لكن من طريق التكلم صحيح، والاستثناء تصرُّفٌ في التكلم بالمنع ، فصح، فكذا هذا، : لما كان تصرُّفاً في حق التكلم صحت الاستعارة به؛ لحكم حقيقته وإن لم ينعقد لإيجاب تلك الحقيقة، ومن حكم الحقيقة : عتقه من حين فجعل إقراراً به، فعَتَقَ في القضاء. بخلاف النداء؛ لأنه لاستحضار المنادَى بصورة الاسم، لا بمعناه، فإذا لم يكن المعنى مطلوباً : لم تجب الاستعارة لتصحيح معناه. بخلاف قوله : يا حر فإنه يستوي فيه نداؤه وخبره؛ لأنه موضوع للتحرير، فصار عينه قائماً مقام معناه، فصار المعنى مطلوباً بكل حال. ومن حكم هذا الباب أن العمل بالحقيقة متى أمكن سقط المجاز؛ لأن المستعار لا يزاحِمُ الأصل. وذلك مثل قولنا في الأقراء : إنها الحيض؛ لأن القُرْء للحيض حقيقة وللطهر مجاز؛ من قبل أنه مأخوذ من الجَمْع، وهو معنى حقيقة هذه العبارة لغةً، وذلك صفة الدم المجتمع. فأما الطهر: فإنما وصف به مجازاً بمجاورته، ولأن معنى القُرء: الانتقالُ، يقال: قرأ النجم: إذا انتقل والانتقال : بالحيض، لا بالطهر، فصارت الحقيقة أولى. وكذلك العقدُ لما ينعقد حقيقةٌ، وللعزم: مجاز، وكذلك النكاح: للجمع في لغة العرب على ما عُرف والاجتماعُ في الوطء، ويسمى العقد نكاحاً مجازاً؛ لأنه سببه ، حتى سُمِّي الوطء جماعاً، فكانت الحقيقة أولى. وأمثلة هذا أكثر من أن تُحصى. ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في الدعوى، في رجل له أمةٌ ولدت ثلاثة أولاد في بطون مختلفة، فقال المولى أحد هؤلاء ولدي، ثم مات قبل البيان: إنه يعتق من كل واحد ثلثه. ولا يُعتبر ما يصيبُ كلَّ واحدٍ منهم من قبل أمه، حتى يعتق الثالث كلّه، ونصفُ الثاني، كما قال أبو يوسف رحمه الله؛ لأن إصابته من قبل أمه في مقابلة إصابته من قِبَل نفسه بمنزلة المجاز من الحقيقة. وأمثلة هذا أكثر من أن تُحصى. وإذا كانت الحقيقة متعذرة أو مهجورة: صير إلى المجاز، بالإجماع؛ لعدم المزاحمة. أما المتعذِّر: فمثلُ الرجل يحلف لا يأكل من هذه النخلة، أو: من هذه الكرمة، أو القدر أنه يقع على ما يُتَّخذُ منه مجازاً. بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذه الشاة، أو: هذا الرطب : فإنه يقع على عينه ؛ لأن الحقيقة قائمة. وكذلك إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذا الدقيق: وقع على ما يتخذ منه؛ لأن الحقيقة متعذرة. وكذلك لو حلف لا يشرب من هذه البئر: لا يقع على الكرع، وهو حقيقة؛ لما قلنا. واختلفوا فيما إذا أكل عين الدقيق، أو تكلف، فكرَعَ من البئر.
And rendering binding more than three [divorces] is invalid in terms of legal effect, but valid in terms of speech. The exception is a disposal in speech by prohibiting, so it is valid, and so is this: because it was a disposal in the right of speech, the metaphor was valid; for the ruling of its reality, even if it does not take effect for the binding of that reality. And from the rulings of reality: his emancipation from the moment he made it an acknowledgment, so he is freed judicially. Contrary to the call [to someone], because it is to summon the called person by the form of the name, not by its meaning; so if the meaning is not sought, the metaphor is not necessary to correct its meaning. Contrary to his saying: O free one, for it is equal in call and narration because it is coined for emancipation, so its essence stands in place of its meaning, so the meaning is sought in any case. And from the rulings of this chapter: that acting upon the literal meaning whenever possible abrogates the metaphorical; because the borrowed does not compete with the original. That is like our saying regarding aqra': that it is menstruation; because aqra' is literal for menstruation and metaphorical for purity; because it is derived from gathering, which is the literal meaning of this expression in language, and that is the description of the collected blood. As for purity: it is described by it metaphorically due to its adjacency, and because the meaning of aqra' is transition; it is said: the star set when it moved, and transition is by menstruation, not by purity, so the literal meaning takes precedence. Likewise, aqd is literal for what is tied, and metaphorical for resolution; likewise, nikah: for union in the Arabic language as known, and the union in intercourse, and the contract is called nikah metaphorically because it is its cause, until intercourse is called jima', so the literal meaning takes precedence. Examples of this are more than can be counted. And for this reason Abu Hanifa said in claims, regarding a man who has a slave girl who gave birth to three children in different pregnancies, and the master said: one of these is my son, then died before clarification: that one third of each is freed. And what pertains to each of them from the mother is not considered, according to what Abu Yusuf said; because his relation from the mother in comparison to his relation from himself is like the metaphorical from the literal. Examples of this are more than can be counted. And when the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, then recourse is to the metaphorical, by consensus, due to the lack of competition. As for the impossible: such as a man swears not to eat from this palm tree, or from this grapevine, or from this pot, that it falls on what is made from it metaphorically. Contrary to if he swears not to eat from this sheep, or this fresh date: it falls on its very self, because the literal meaning exists. Likewise, if he swears not to eat from this flour: it falls on what is made from it, because the literal meaning is impossible. Likewise, if he swears not to drink from this well: it does not fall on lapping [directly], which is literal, for what we said. And they differed regarding if one eats the flour itself, or struggles and laps from the well.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal meaning of a word or statement.
المجاز (al-majaz): A metaphorical or secondary meaning that is not the primary literal one.

Application of the Rule in Oaths: When Haqiqah is Impossible
Explaining the exception to the haqiqah rule in cases of impossibility, with practical oath examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when haqiqah is impossible or abandoned, majaz is used by consensus, by analyzing oath examples from the text.

Core Idea
When literal meaning fails, metaphor saves the oath.

This lesson examines the exception to the haqiqah-over-majaz rule. When the literal meaning of a word in an oath is impossible or abandoned by custom, Muslims agree that the metaphorical meaning takes over. We will explore several oath scenarios to see how this exception works in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the exception
When haqiqah is impossible (تعذر) or abandoned (مهجور), then recourse to majaz is allowed by consensus. This is because there is no competition from the literal meaning.
This is a unanimous rule (with consensus) across schools.
2
Examine the example of an oath about a palm tree
If a man swears not to eat from this palm tree, the literal meaning (eating the wood/bark) is impossible. So the oath is interpreted metaphorically: he means not to eat the dates from that tree.
Similarly for grapevine (wine/grapes) and pot (contents).
3
Contrast with cases where haqiqah is possible
If he swears not to eat from this sheep (the animal), the literal meaning is possible (eating the meat), so the oath applies to the sheep itself. Similarly for fresh dates.
The literal meaning is possible, so metaphor is not used.
4
Analyze the flour and well examples
Swearing not to eat from this flour: literally, flour cannot be eaten directly (it is raw). So the metaphorical meaning (bread made from it) applies. Swearing not to drink from this well: literal meaning (lapping the water from the well) is impossible, so the oath applies to the water drawn from it.
These show impossibility of direct literal action.
5
Note the ikhtilaf on boundary cases
The text mentions a disagreement about whether eating the actual flour itself (uncommon but possible) breaks the oath. This shows that even with impossibility, there can be marginal disputes.
The rule is clear, but its application at the edges may vary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
متعذرة (mut'addhira): Impossible or extremely difficult in a way that renders literal meaning impractical.
مهجورة (mahjura): Abandoned by common usage; the literal meaning is no longer used in practice.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition do Hanafi scholars unanimously allow the use of metaphorical meaning (majaz) in oaths?
A) When the literal meaning is ambiguous.
B) When the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned.
C) When the metaphorical meaning is more common.
D) When the oath taker is unaware of the literal meaning.

Key Takeaway
The rule that haqiqah takes precedence over majaz has an exception: when haqiqah is impossible or abandoned, majaz is used by consensus.

The Scope of Haqiqah vs Majaz in Oaths
Intra-madhhab dispute on priority of literal meaning in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Abu Hanifa's position from the Sahibayn's on the application of literal vs metaphorical meaning in oaths and explain the usul principle behind the difference.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa restricts oaths to literal meanings; the Sahibayn extend them metaphorically—the key is where preponderance is considered: speech-act or ruling.

This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of literal and metaphorical meanings in legal oaths. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that only the literal meaning binds the oath-taker, while his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, allow metaphorical extension. The debate centers on a subtle usul principle: whether the preponderance of the metaphorical meaning is considered in the act of speaking or in the legal ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Recognize that the dispute concerns whether an oath applies to the literal referent (haqiqah) or can extend to metaphorical meanings (majaz). The context is oaths involving food and drink.
The source explicitly contrasts 'haqiqah' and 'majaz'.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and His Usul Principle
Abu Hanifa holds that the preponderance (rujhan) of majaz is only in the speech-act (al-takallum), not in the legal ruling (al-hukm). Therefore, the literal meaning determines the legal consequence of the oath.
The text says: 'من أصل أبي حنيفة: أنه خَلَفٌ في التكلم، دون الحكم'.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Their Rationale
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that majaz has preponderance because it encompasses both literal and metaphorical, thus including the ruling of the literal and being more deserving. Hence, the oath applies to the substance beyond the literal grain or riverbank.
The text says: 'فصار مشتملاً على حكم الحقيقة، فصار أولى'.
4
Analyze the Thamarah al-Khilaf via the Examples
Work through the two examples: (1) Swearing not to eat 'this wheat': Abu Hanifa limits to the grain; Sahibayn extend to all products. (2) Swearing not to drink from the Euphrates: Abu Hanifa limits to drinking directly at the riverbank; Sahibayn include water drawn from adjacent areas.
The practical difference is whether the oath is violated by secondary uses.
5
Connect to the Usul Principle
The core disagreement is whether preponderance of majaz is considered in the speech-act (Abu Hanifa) or in the ruling (Sahibayn). This principle governs how other ambiguous oaths are resolved in Hanafi fiqh.
Recall the maxim: 'Al-ibrah fi al-yamin bi al-urf' – but here the usul principle overrides custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحقيقة في حق الحكم وفي الحكم للمجاز رجحان؛ لأنه ينطلق على الحقيقة والمجاز معاً ، فصار مشتملاً على حكم الحقيقة، فصار أولى. ومن أصل أبي حنيفة رحمه الله : أنه خَلَفٌ في التكلّم، دون الحكم، فاعتبر الرجحان في التكلم، دون الحكم، فصارت الحقيقة أولى. مثاله : مَن حلف لا يأكل من هذه الحنطة : يقع على عينها، دون ما يتخذ منها عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله ؛ لما قلنا، وعندهما: يقع على مضمونها على العموم مجازاً. وكذلك إذا حلف لا يشرب من الفرات: يقع على الكَرْع خاصةً عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وعندهما: يقع على شرب ماء مجاور للفرات، وذلك لا ينقطع بالأواني ؛ لأنها دون النهر في الإمساك، والله أعلم.
The literal meaning (haqiqah) is superior in ruling, and in the ruling the metaphorical (majaz) has preponderance; because it applies to both the literal and the metaphorical together, so it includes the ruling of the literal, thus it becomes more deserving. And from the principle of Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him): that there is a substitution in the speech-act, not in the ruling, so he considered the preponderance in the speech-act, not in the ruling, thus the literal became more deserving. Example: Whoever swears not to eat of this wheat: it applies to its grain, not to what is made from it according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) due to what we said. And according to both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): it applies to its substance universally as a metaphor. Similarly, if he swears not to drink from the Euphrates: it applies specifically to the riverbank according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). And according to both: it applies to drinking water adjacent to the Euphrates, and that is not prevented by vessels because they are below the river in containment. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-madhhab dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, concerning the application of literal (haqiqah) versus metaphorical (majaz) meanings in the context of legal oaths. The author first states a general principle: the literal meaning has priority in the ruling, but the metaphorical has a preponderance in a certain sense because it encompasses both meanings. However, from Abu Hanifa's principle, this preponderance is only considered in the act of speaking (التكلم), not in the legal ruling (الحكم). Hence, the literal meaning takes priority in determining the legal effect. Two examples illustrate the difference: (1) If someone swears not to eat 'this wheat,' Abu Hanifa holds the oath applies only to the grain itself, not to its products (e.g., bread), while the companions hold it applies metaphorically to all substances derived from it. (2) If someone swears not to drink from the Euphrates, Abu Hanifa restricts it to drinking directly from the riverbank (الكَرْع), whereas the companions extend it to water drawn from adjacent areas, as the oath is about the water source, not the act of drawing. The companions' reasoning is that the river's water can be accessed via vessels, so the oath includes that. The passage ends with 'والله أعلم', indicating the author's deference to divine knowledge.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): The literal or original meaning of a word as established by linguistic convention.
المجاز (al-majāz): The metaphorical or transferred meaning of a word, used due to a relationship with the literal meaning.
الرجحان (al-rujḥān): Preponderance or superiority of one meaning over another in a specific context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The oath applies only to the grain itself according to Abu Hanifa.
B) The oath applies to all products of wheat according to Abu Hanifa.
C) The oath applies only to the grain itself according to the Sahibayn.
D) The oath applies to both grain and products according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, for oaths, the literal meaning (haqiqah) takes precedence because preponderance of metaphorical meaning (majaz) is only considered in the speech-act, not in the legal ruling.

Five Reasons for Abandoning Literal Meaning (Haqiqah)
Classification of reasons for abandoning haqiqah with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five reasons for abandoning haqiqah and explain how the first reason (indication of usage and custom) operates using the examples of salah and hajj.

Core Idea
When custom overwhelms usage, the metaphorical becomes the literal.

In Hanafi usul, the default assumption is that words carry their literal meaning (haqiqah). However, this literal meaning can be abandoned when there is a stronger indication pointing to a metaphorical meaning (majaz). The passage you will study enumerates five such indications. The first and most frequently encountered is the indication of common usage and custom (dalālat al-istiʿmāl wa al-ʿādah), illustrated by the words 'salat' and 'hajj' whose literal meanings (supplication and intention) have been superseded by their well-known Shar'i meanings.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the five categories
Read the five reasons listed: (1) usage/custom, (2) the word itself, (3) context, (4) the speaker, (5) subject matter. Commit these to memory as a framework for analyzing khiṭāb.
Focus on the first one for now; the rest will be explained later.
2
Understand the first reason
The first reason is 'indication of usage and custom'. It means that when a word is used so often in a non-literal sense that people no longer think of the literal meaning, the haqiqah is abandoned. The passage gives the example of 'salat' and 'hajj'.
The Quran itself uses 'salat' both literally (supplication) and metaphorically (ritual prayer).
3
Analyze the example of salah
The literal meaning of salah is du'a (supplication). The Quran uses it literally in 'wa ṣalli ʿalayhim' (pray for them). But the Shar'i meaning (ritual prayer) became dominant because it involves dhikr, a form of supplication. Thus, the metaphorical meaning took over.
The shift is supported by the Quranic verse 'aqimi l-ṣalāta li-dhikrī'.
4
Extend to other examples
Similarly, 'hajj' literally means 'intention' or 'resolve', but came to denote the pilgrimage because of the strong intention to travel. 'Umrah' and 'zakat' follow the same pattern. Recognize that when a metaphorical usage becomes universal, it effectively becomes the new literal meaning in legal discourse.
This principle shows that language is shaped by the needs of the community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو خمسة أنواع : باب جملة ما تترك به الحقيقة -١- قد تُترك بدلالة الاستعمال والعادة. ٢- وقد تُترك بدلالة اللفظ في نفسه. وقد تُترك بدلالة سياق النظم. ٤ـ وقد تترك بدلالة ترجع إلى المتكلم. ه وقد تُترك بدلالة في محل الكلام ١ - أما الأول : فمثل الصلاة، فإنها اسم للدعاء قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَصَلِ عَلَيْهِمْ التوبة / ۱۰۳ : أي ادعُ، ثم سُمِّي بها عبادة معلومة مجازاً؛ لما أنها شرعت للذكر، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَأَقِمِ الصَّلَوٰةَ لِذِكْرِي ﴾ طه/١٤ ، وكلَّ ن ذكر دعاء . وكالحج، فإنه قصد في اللغة، وصار اسماً لعبادة معلومة مجازاً؛ لما فيها من قوة العزيمة والقصد، بقطع المسافة. وكذلك نظائرها من العمرة والزكاة، حتى صارت الحقيقة مهجورة. وإنما صار هذا دلالة على ترك الحقيقة؛ لأن الكلام موضوع لاستعمال الناس وحاجتهم ، فيصير المجاز باستعمالهم كالحقيقة.
And it is five types: Chapter: The totality of reasons for abandoning the literal meaning. 1) It may be abandoned by indication of usage and custom. 2) And it may be abandoned by indication of the word itself. 3) And it may be abandoned by indication of the context (siyaq al-nazm). 4) And it may be abandoned by indication attributed to the speaker. 5) And it may be abandoned by indication in the subject of the speech. 1) As for the first: for example, salah: it is a name for supplication. Allah Almighty said: 'And pray for them' (At-Tawbah 9:103), meaning supplicate. Then it was named for a known act of worship metaphorically, because it was prescribed for remembrance. Allah Almighty said: 'And establish prayer for My remembrance' (Taha 20:14), and every remembrance is a supplication. And like hajj: it means intent in language, and became a name for a known act of worship metaphorically, because of the strength of resolve and intention in covering distance. And similar examples like umrah and zakat, until the literal meaning became abandoned. And this serves as an indication for abandoning the literal meaning, because speech is set up for the usage and need of people, so the metaphorical becomes like the literal through their usage.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a classification of five reasons why the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word may be abandoned in favor of a metaphorical meaning (majaz). These reasons are: (1) indication of usage and custom (الاستعمال والعادة), (2) indication of the word itself (اللفظ في نفسه), (3) indication of context (سياق النظم), (4) indication of the speaker (المتكلم), and (5) indication of the subject matter (محل الكلام). The author then elaborates on the first type with examples: the word 'salat' originally means supplication (du'a), as in the Quranic verse 'وصل عليهم' (pray for them). It then came to be used metaphorically for the ritual prayer because it involves remembrance (dhikr), which is a kind of supplication. Similarly, 'hajj' originally means 'intention' but is used for the pilgrimage due to the strong resolve and aiming to cover distance. Likewise, 'umrah' and 'zakat' are examples where the metaphorical usage became so dominant that the literal meaning fell into disuse. The underlying principle is that language is tailored to people's usage and needs; thus, when metaphorical usage becomes widespread, it attains the status of literal meaning.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): The literal meaning of a word as originally intended by language.
المجاز (al-majāz): The metaphorical meaning of a word, commonly used in place of the literal due to a relationship.
دلالة الاستعمال والعادة (dalālat al-istiʿmāl wa al-ʿādah): Indication from common usage and customary practice that a word has a metaphorical meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The word's internal linguistic structure.
B) The context of the surrounding verses.
C) The widespread usage and custom of the people.
D) The intention of the original speaker.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the literal meaning of a word may be abandoned when common usage and custom establish a metaphorical meaning as the dominant one.
