The Primary Meaning of 'Fa': Immediate Succession
Defining the primary meaning of 'fa' and its immediate legal effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that 'fa' indicates immediate succession to analyze legal statements in contracts and conditions.

Core Idea
The letter 'fa' in legal Arabic always implies immediate succession—even a slight delay changes the ruling.

This lesson examines the foundational meaning of the conjunction 'fa' in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Understanding this is crucial for interpreting conditions, contracts, and causal links. We will see how early Hanafi scholars used this principle to derive rulings in sales and divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Al-Bazdawi defines 'fa' as indicating 'wusul' (connection) and 'ta'qib' (immediate succession). The conjunct follows the object of conjunction without significant delay.
Even a subtle delay is possible, but the essential meaning is sequential.
2
Distinguish 'fa' from 'waw'
Unlike 'waw' (simple conjunction), 'fa' imposes a temporal order. This is why Arabs use 'fa' in conditional clauses (جزاء) where the result necessarily follows the condition.
3
Apply to sale acceptance
If a buyer says 'I sold you this slave' and the other replies 'fa-huwa hurr' (then he is free), this constitutes acceptance of the sale because 'fa' makes the freeing immediate.
If 'waw' or no conjunction were used, the sale would not be valid.
4
Apply to divorce
In 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, divorced' (with 'fa' implied in the repetition), the divorces occur in sequence. The first divorce becomes irrevocable (bain) immediately after entry.
The sequence is directed to the obligation, not the obligatory.
5
Understand the debt example
When someone says 'For so-and-so upon me is a dirham, fa dirham' (a dirham, then a dirham), two dirhams are obligatory because 'fa' connects separate obligations.
The sequence applies to the obligation (wujub), not the owed amount.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حرف الفاء :] وأما الفاء : فإنه للوصل والتعقيب حتى إن المعطوف بالفاء يتراخى عن المعطوف عليه بزمان وإن لطف، هذا موجبه الذي وضع له، ألا يُرى أن العرب تستعمل الفاء في الجزاء؛ لأنه مرتب لا محالة. وتُستعمل في أحكام العلل، كما يقال: جاء الشتاء فتأهب ؛ لأن الحكم مرتب على العلة. ويقال : أخذتُ كلَّ ثوب من هذا العدل بعشرة فصاعداً: أي كان كذلك، فازداد الثمن صاعداً مرتفعاً. ولما قلنا إن وجوه العطف منقسمة على صلاته، فلا بد من أن يكون الفاء مختصاً بمعنى هو موضوع له حقيقةً، وذلك هو التعقيب، ولذلك قال أصحابنا رحمهم الله : فيمن قال لآخر بعتُ منك هذا العبد بكذا، فقال الآخر: فهو حر : إنه قبول للبيع. ولو قال : هو حر، أو : وهو حر : لم يجز البيع. قال مشايخنا فيمن قال لخيّاط : انظر إلى هذا الثوب أيكفيني قميصاً؟ فنظر، فقال: نعم، فقال : فاقطعه ، فقَطَعَه ، فإذا هو لا يكفيه : إنه يضمن. كما لو قال : فإن كفاني قميصاً : فاقطعه، فقَطَعَه ، يضمن. فإذا هو لا يكفيه : إنه يضمن. ولذلك قالوا فيمن قال لامرأته : إن دخلت الدارَ فأنت طالقٌ فطالقٌ، فدخلت الدار وهي غير مدخول بها : إنه يقع على الترتيب، فتبين بالأُولى. ولذلك اختص الفاء بعطف الحكم على العلل، كما يقال: أطعمته فأشبعته : أي بهذا الإطعام. وقال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : لن يُجزىء ولد والده حتى يجده مملوكاً، فيشتريه، فيُعتقه ) ، فدل ذلك على أن كونه معتقاً : حكم للشراء بواسطة الملك. ولهذا قلنا فيمن قال : إن دخلت هذه الدار فهذه الدار فعبدي حرَّ: إن الشرط أن تدخل الأخيرة بعد الأولى من غير تراخ. وقد تدخل الفاء على العلل أيضاً إذا كان ذلك مما يدوم، فتصير بمعنى المتراخي، كما يقال : أبشر فقد أتاك الغَوْثُ، وقد نجوت. ونظيره : ما قال علماؤنا رحمهم الله في : المأذون فيمن قال لعبده : أد : إليَّ ألفاً فأنت حر : إنه يعتق للحال، وتقديره : أد إلي ألفاً فإنك قد عتقت؛ لأن العتق دائم، فأشبه المتراخي. وقالوا : في السَّير الكبير»: انزِلْ فأنتَ آمِنُ : إنه آمِنُ: نزل أو لم ينزل؛ لما قلنا، فلم يُجعل بمعنى التعليق كأنه أضمر الشرط؛ لأن الكلام صح بدون الإضمار. وإنما الإضمار ضروري في الأصل، ولهذا قلنا : فيمن قال : لفلان علي درهم فدرهم: إنه يلزمه درهمان؛ لأن المعطوف غير الأول، ويُصرف الترتيب إلى الوجوب، دون الواجب، أو يُجعل مستعاراً (۱) حديث : لن يَجزي ولد والده عن أبي هريرة قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لا يجزي ولد والده إلا أن يجده مملوكاً فيشتريه، فيعتقه». رواه الجماعة إلا البخاري.
[The letter 'fa':] As for 'fa', it is for connection and immediate succession such that the conjunct with 'fa' is delayed from the object of conjunction by a time even if subtle. This is its essential meaning for which it was coined. Do you not see that the Arabs use 'fa' in the protasis because it is necessarily sequential. And it is used in rulings of causes, as it is said: 'Winter came, so prepare'; because the ruling is dependent on the cause. And it is said: 'I took each garment from this bundle for ten, and upward': meaning it was so, and the price increased upward. And since we say that the aspects of conjunction are divided according to its contexts, it is necessary that 'fa' be specific to a meaning for which it is literally coined, and that is immediate succession. Therefore, our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) said: regarding one who said to another 'I sold you this slave for such', and the other said 'then he is free': that it is acceptance of the sale. And if he said 'he is free' or 'and he is free', the sale is not valid. Our teachers said regarding one who said to a tailor: 'Look at this cloth, will it suffice me for a shirt?' He looked and said 'yes', then said 'then cut it', and he cut it, and it did not suffice him: that he is liable. Just as if he said: 'If it suffices me for a shirt, then cut it', and he cut it, and it did not suffice him: he is liable. Therefore, they said regarding one who said to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, divorced', and she entered the house while she was not yet consummated: that it occurs in sequence, and she becomes irrevocably divorced by the first. Therefore, 'fa' is specifically for connecting the ruling to its cause, as it is said: 'I fed him, so he became full': meaning by this feeding. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'A son will not repay his father unless he finds him a slave, buys him, and frees him.' So this proves that his being a free person is a ruling of the purchase through ownership. Therefore, we said regarding one who said: 'If you enter this house, then this house, then my slave is free': that the condition is that she enter the last after the first without delay. And 'fa' may also be used for causes if that which is continuous, then it becomes in the sense of delay, as it is said: 'Rejoice, for relief has come to you, and you have been saved.' And its analogue: what our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) said regarding the authorized slave: in the case of one who said to his slave: 'Pay me a thousand, then you are free': that he becomes free immediately, and its estimation is: 'Pay me a thousand, for you have been freed'; because freedom is continuous, so it resembles the delayed. And they said in 'al-Siyar al-Kabir': 'Descend, and you are safe' means he is safe whether he descends or not; because of what we said, so it is not made in the sense of a condition as if he implied the condition; because the speech is sound without implication. And implication is necessary only originally. Therefore, we said regarding one who said: 'To so-and-so upon me is a dirham and a dirham': that two dirhams are obligatory because the conjunct is other than the first, and the sequence is directed to the obligation, not the obligatory, or it is made metaphorical. (1) Hadith: 'A son will not repay his father' from Abu Hurairah: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'A son will not repay his father unless he finds him a slave, buys him, and frees him.' Narrated by all except al-Bukhari.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the Arabic letter 'fa' (ف) as used in legal language. The author, al-Bazdawi, begins by defining its primary meaning: immediate succession (تعقيب). He argues that even a minimal delay distinguishes 'fa' from other conjunctions like 'waw'. He supports this by noting Arab usage in conditional clauses (الجزاء) where the consequence follows the condition necessarily. Next, he applies this to legal rulings: for instance, in a sale, the word 'fa' in 'then he is free' indicates immediate acceptance, whereas 'and he is free' would not. The tailor example shows that ‘fa’ in a command (فاقطعه) implies immediate action, making the tailor liable if the cloth does not suffice. Similarly, in divorce, ‘fa’ causes successive divorces. The hadith about freeing a father proves that emancipation (عتق) is a ruling resulting from purchase via ownership. The passage also notes exceptions: when the result is continuous (like freedom), ‘fa’ can imply a delayed effect, as in ‘Descend, and you are safe’ where safety is assured regardless of descent. Finally, in debts, ‘fa’ indicates separate obligations due to the conjunction of distinct items.

Key Terms
التعقيب (al-ta'qib): Immediate succession; the primary meaning of 'fa' is to indicate that the conjunct follows without significant delay.
عطف الحكم على العلل (atf al-hukm 'ala al-'ilal): Connecting a legal ruling to its cause, showing that the ruling is a direct consequence of the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary meaning of the letter 'fa' (ف) in legal usage?
A) Simple conjunction without any temporal order.
B) Immediate succession (تعقيب) even if the delay is minimal.
C) Causal connection only.
D) Alternative (تخيير) between two options.

Key Takeaway
The primary meaning of 'fa' is immediate succession, and this has direct legal consequences in contracts and conditions.

Legal Implications and Exceptions: Continuous Results and Causal Links
Exploring legal applications and exceptions to the immediate succession rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when 'fa' indicates immediate vs. delayed succession based on the nature of the legal consequence.

Core Idea
When a legal consequence is continuous (like freedom or safety), 'fa' can lose its immediacy and permit a delayed effect.

This lesson explores the exception to the general rule: when the outcome of a condition or cause is continuous in nature, the conjunction 'fa' may not imply immediate execution. We examine the hadith on freeing a father, the tailor liability case, and the analogies from the authorized slave and safe passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tailor example
A man tells a tailor 'If the cloth suffices, then cut it.' The tailor cuts immediately but it doesn't suffice. The tailor is liable because 'fa' commands immediate action.
2
Identify the hadith
The Prophet (PBUH) said: 'A son will not repay his father unless he finds him a slave, buys him, and frees him.' The 'fa' in 'fayashtarihi, fa-yu'tiqahu' shows that freeing is a ruling of purchase via ownership.
The sequence: purchase -> ownership -> emancipation.
3
Recognize the causal link
The passage states that 'fa' is specifically for connecting rulings to causes (عطف الحكم على العلل). The hadith demonstrates this: the ruling (freedom) is a consequence of the cause (purchase).
4
Analyze the exception for continuous results
When the result is continuous (e.g., freedom, safety), 'fa' can imply a delayed effect. The authorized slave example: 'Pay me a thousand, then you are free' - the slave becomes free immediately, but freedom is continuous, so it resembles a delayed consequence.
Freedom is a continuous state, so the 'fa' no longer strictly requires immediate execution of the freeing.
5
Apply to the 'descend, you are safe' case
In 'al-Siyar al-Kabir', 'Descend, and you are safe' means safety is granted whether he descends or not, because 'fa' here indicates a continuous state (safety) and not a conditional immediate consequence.
The ruling is based on the principle that continuous outcomes allow delay.

[The letter 'fa':] As for 'fa', it is for connection and immediate succession such that the conjunct with 'fa' is delayed from the object of conjunction by a time even if subtle. This is its essential meaning for which it was coined. Do you not see that the Arabs use 'fa' in the protasis because it is necessarily sequential. And it is used in rulings of causes, as it is said: 'Winter came, so prepare'; because the ruling is dependent on the cause. And it is said: 'I took each garment from this bundle for ten, and upward': meaning it was so, and the price increased upward. And since we say that the aspects of conjunction are divided according to its contexts, it is necessary that 'fa' be specific to a meaning for which it is literally coined, and that is immediate succession. Therefore, our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) said: regarding one who said to another 'I sold you this slave for such', and the other said 'then he is free': that it is acceptance of the sale. And if he said 'he is free' or 'and he is free', the sale is not valid. Our teachers said regarding one who said to a tailor: 'Look at this cloth, will it suffice me for a shirt?' He looked and said 'yes', then said 'then cut it', and he cut it, and it did not suffice him: that he is liable. Just as if he said: 'If it suffices me for a shirt, then cut it', and he cut it, and it did not suffice him: he is liable. Therefore, they said regarding one who said to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, divorced', and she entered the house while she was not yet consummated: that it occurs in sequence, and she becomes irrevocably divorced by the first. Therefore, 'fa' is specifically for connecting the ruling to its cause, as it is said: 'I fed him, so he became full': meaning by this feeding. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'A son will not repay his father unless he finds him a slave, buys him, and frees him.' So this proves that his being a free person is a ruling of the purchase through ownership. Therefore, we said regarding one who said: 'If you enter this house, then this house, then my slave is free': that the condition is that she enter the last after the first without delay. And 'fa' may also be used for causes if that which is continuous, then it becomes in the sense of delay, as it is said: 'Rejoice, for relief has come to you, and you have been saved.' And its analogue: what our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) said regarding the authorized slave: in the case of one who said to his slave: 'Pay me a thousand, then you are free': that he becomes free immediately, and its estimation is: 'Pay me a thousand, for you have been freed'; because freedom is continuous, so it resembles the delayed. And they said in 'al-Siyar al-Kabir': 'Descend, and you are safe' means he is safe whether he descends or not; because of what we said, so it is not made in the sense of a condition as if he implied the condition; because the speech is sound without implication. And implication is necessary only originally. Therefore, we said regarding one who said: 'To so-and-so upon me is a dirham and a dirham': that two dirhams are obligatory because the conjunct is other than the first, and the sequence is directed to the obligation, not the obligatory, or it is made metaphorical. (1) Hadith: 'A son will not repay his father' from Abu Hurairah: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'A son will not repay his father unless he finds him a slave, buys him, and frees him.' Narrated by all except al-Bukhari.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the Arabic letter 'fa' (ف) as used in legal language. The author, al-Bazdawi, begins by defining its primary meaning: immediate succession (تعقيب). He argues that even a minimal delay distinguishes 'fa' from other conjunctions like 'waw'. He supports this by noting Arab usage in conditional clauses (الجزاء) where the consequence follows the condition necessarily. Next, he applies this to legal rulings: for instance, in a sale, the word 'fa' in 'then he is free' indicates immediate acceptance, whereas 'and he is free' would not. The tailor example shows that ‘fa’ in a command (فاقطعه) implies immediate action, making the tailor liable if the cloth does not suffice. Similarly, in divorce, ‘fa’ causes successive divorces. The hadith about freeing a father proves that emancipation (عتق) is a ruling resulting from purchase via ownership. The passage also notes exceptions: when the result is continuous (like freedom), ‘fa’ can imply a delayed effect, as in ‘Descend, and you are safe’ where safety is assured regardless of descent. Finally, in debts, ‘fa’ indicates separate obligations due to the conjunction of distinct items.

Key Terms
التعقيب (al-ta'qib): Immediate succession; the primary meaning of 'fa' is to indicate that the conjunct follows without significant delay.
عطف الحكم على العلل (atf al-hukm 'ala al-'ilal): Connecting a legal ruling to its cause, showing that the ruling is a direct consequence of the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When used in a sale acceptance.
B) When the outcome is continuous (e.g., freedom or safety).
C) When used in a conditional divorce.
D) When used in the hadith about freeing a father.

Key Takeaway
When the legal outcome is continuous (like freedom or safety), 'fa' can permit a delayed effect, diverging from its primary meaning of immediate succession.

Metaphorical Use of 'Thumma' as 'Waw': When Literal Meaning is Impossible
Analyzing the metaphorical interpretation of 'ثم' as 'واو' based on impossibility of literal meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that when the literal meaning (haqiqa) of a word leads to legal impossibility, it is interpreted metaphorically (majaz), and identify such cases in legal texts.

Core Idea
When literal meaning is legally impossible, metaphorical interpretation becomes necessary.

Legal texts are interpreted literally by default, but what happens when the literal meaning leads to an impossible or impermissible outcome? The Hanafi school recognizes that in such cases, a metaphorical meaning (majaz) must be adopted to achieve the intended legal purpose. This lesson examines the particle 'thumma' (then) in a hadith on expiation and shows how the impossibility of acting upon its literal meaning forces a metaphorical reading as 'waw' (and).

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning (haqiqa) of 'thumma' in the hadith
The hadith in one version says: '...then expiate his oath.' Literally, 'thumma' implies sequence with a delay. Determine what action the literal meaning requires.
2
Assess feasibility of literal implementation
Check whether acting upon the literal meaning is legally possible. Here, expiation is only obligatory after violating an oath, not before. Thus literal 'then' would require expiation before the better deed, which is not legally obligatory.
If the literal meaning were possible, it would be taken; impossibility triggers metaphorical interpretation.
3
Apply the principle of majaz when haqiqa is impossible
Since literal implementation is impossible, interpret 'thumma' metaphorically as 'waw' (and), making the actions concurrent or without sequence. This ensures the ruling aligns with legal obligations.
The metaphorical meaning must be conventionally recognized (majaz 'urfi).
4
Verify with other narrations and Quranic evidence
The passage cites Quranic verses where 'thumma' is used metaphorically (e.g., 'Then he was among those who believed' meaning 'and') and other hadith versions that use 'waw' explicitly, confirming the validity of the metaphorical reading.
Multiple versions support the interpretation.
5
Extend the principle to similar particles (e.g., 'fa')
The author notes that if 'thumma' can be used metaphorically for 'waw', then 'fa' (then) is even more likely, due to closer semantic adjacency. This principle applies to other legal contexts, such as conditional divorce using 'fa'.
The same reasoning applies to similar constructions.

Source Text

قال: إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق طالق طالق. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : يتعلقن جميعاً، وينزلن على الترتيب، سواء قدم الشرط أو أخر. ولو كانت مدخولاً بها : نزل الأول والثاني، وتعلق الثالث إذا أخر الشرط، وإذا قدمه : تعلق الأولُ ، ونزل الباقي عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وعندهما يتعلق الكل. ذكره في «النوادر». وقد يُستعار : ثم : بمعنى: واو العطف مجازاً؛ للمجاورة التي بينهما، قال الله تعالى: ﴿ ثُمَّ كَانَ مِنَ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا ﴾ [البلد/17]، ﴿ ثُمَّ اللَّهُ شَهِيدٌ عَلَى مَا يَفْعَلُونَ ﴾ [يونس/46]. ولهذا قلنا في قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «من حلف على يمين، فرأى غيرها خيراً منها : فليأت بالذي هو خيرٌ ، ثم ليكفر عن يمينه». وله ألفاظ : فعن عبد الرحمن بن سمرة قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إذا حلفت على يمين فرأيت غيرها خيراً منها : فأت الذي هو خير، وكفر يمينك»، وفي لفظ: «فكفر عن يمينك، وأت الذي هو خير»، متفق عليهما، وفي لفظ : «إذا حلفت على يمين : فكفر عن يمينك، ثم أت الذي هو خير»، رواه النسائي وأبو داود. وعن عدي بن حاتم قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إذا حلف أحدكم على اليمين فرأى خيراً منها فليكفرها ، وليأت الذي هو خير». رواه مسلم، إنه يُحمل على حقيقته؛ لأن العمل به ممكن؛ لأنا نعمل بحقيقة موجب الأمر، فنجعل الكفارة واجبةً بعد الحنث. - وروي: «فليكفر يمينه، ثم ليأت بالذي هو خير»: فحملنا هذا على واو العطف؛ لأن العمل بحقيقته غير ممكن، وهو موجب الأمر؛ لأن التكفير قبل الحنث غير واجب، فكان المجاز متعيّناً؛ تحقيقاً لما المقصود. وإذا صح أن يُستعار ثم للواو فالفاء به أولى؛ لأن جوازه بالفاء أقرب، ولهذا قال بعض مشايخنا رحمهم الله : فيمن قال لامرأته : إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق فطالقٌ فطالقٌ، ولم يدخل بها: إن هذا على الاختلاف، مثل ما اختلفوا في الواو ، إلا أن الحقيقة أولى، فلذلك اخترنا الاتفاق في هذا. لفظ من حلف على يمين فرأى غيرها خيراً منها، فليأت الذي هو خير، وليكفر عن يمينه. رواه أحمد ومسلم والنسائي وابن ماجه وهذا مثل لفظ الكتاب، إلا أنه بالواو. وعن أبي هريرة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «من حلف على يمين، فرأى غيرها خيراً منها فليكفر عن يمينه وليفعل الذي هو خير». رواه أحمد ومسلم والترمذي وصححه، وفي لفظ: «فليأت الذي هو خير وليكفر عن يمينه». رواه مسلم. وعن أبي موسى الأشعري عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «لا أحلف على يمين فأرى غيرها خيراً منها : إلا أتيت الذي هو خير وتحللتها»، وفي لفظ : «إلا أتيت الذي هو خير، وكفرت عن يميني»، وفي لفظ : «إلا كفرت عن يميني، وأتيت الذي هو خير» متفق عليهن.
He said: If you enter the house, you are divorced, divorced, divorced. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: All of them are attached, and they occur in order, whether the condition is placed first or last. If she was one with whom he had consummated marriage: the first and second occur, and the third is attached when he delays the condition; and when he precedes it, the first is attached and the rest occur according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). According to them, all are attached. He mentioned it in al-Nawadir. And 'thumma' is sometimes used metaphorically in the meaning of the conjunction 'waw' due to the adjacency between them. Allah Almighty said: {Then he was among those who believed} [al-Balad 17], {Then Allah is witness over what they do} [Yunus 46]. For this reason we said concerning the Prophet's statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever swears an oath and sees something better than it, let him do what is better, then expiate his oath.' And there are versions: from Abd al-Rahman ibn Samura said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'If you swear an oath and see something better than it, do what is better and expiate your oath,' and in a version: 'Expiate your oath and do what is better,' both agreed upon, and in a version: 'If you swear an oath, expiate your oath, then do what is better,' narrated by al-Nasa'i and Abu Dawud. And from Adi ibn Hatim said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'If one of you swears an oath and sees something better than it, let him expiate it and do what is better.' Narrated by Muslim. It is taken according to its literal meaning because it is possible to act upon it; we act upon the literal meaning of the command, so we make expiation obligatory after the violation. And it is narrated: 'Let him expiate his oath, then do what is better' – we interpreted this as the conjunction 'waw' because acting upon its literal meaning is not possible, and it is a command, because expiation before violation is not obligatory, so the metaphorical meaning is necessary to achieve the intended purpose. And if it is established that 'thumma' can be used metaphorically for 'waw', then 'fa' is even more fitting because its permissibility as 'fa' is closer. For this reason, some of our shaykhs (may Allah have mercy on them) said: concerning someone who says to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced' and he has not consummated with her: this comes under the same difference of opinion as they differed regarding 'waw', except that the literal meaning is more deserving, so we chose agreement in this. The wording: 'Whoever swears an oath and sees something better than it, let him do what is better and expiate his oath.' Narrated by Ahmad, Muslim, al-Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah, and this is like the wording of the book, except that it uses 'waw'. And from Abu Hurayra that the Prophet said: 'Whoever swears an oath and sees something better than it, let him expiate his oath and do what is better.' Narrated by Ahmad, Muslim, al-Tirmidhi and he declared it sound, and in a version: 'let him do what is better and expiate his oath.' Narrated by Muslim. And from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari from the Prophet: 'I do not swear an oath and see something better than it except that I do what is better and release myself from it,' and in a version: 'except that I do what is better and expiate my oath,' and in a version: 'except that I expiate my oath and do what is better' – all agreed upon.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a usul principle: when the literal meaning of a word or phrase is impossible to implement due to legal constraints, it may be interpreted metaphorically to achieve the intended legal purpose. The author begins with a classical Hanafi dispute regarding divorce by three consecutive pronouncements. He presents the ruling of Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on the case where a husband says to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, divorced, divorced.' The Sahibayn hold that all three are attached but occur in order; Abu Hanifa differentiates based on whether the wife was previously consummated and whether the condition is placed before or after. The Sahibayn's position is recorded in al-Nawadir, indicating it is a less authoritative transmission. The author then pivots to the linguistic analysis of the particle 'thumma' (then). He notes that 'thumma' is sometimes metaphorically used to mean 'waw' (and) due to their semantic adjacency, citing Quranic verses as evidence. This principle is applied to a hadith on oaths: the Prophet said 'then expiate' but other versions use 'and'. The author explains that when literal meaning is possible (as in the majority version where expiation comes after the better act), we take it literally. However, in the version 'then expiate' followed by 'then do better', the literal meaning would require expiation before the act, which is not legally obligatory, so we interpret 'thumma' as 'waw' to make it consistent. This is a classic example of usul analysis where impossibility of literal application forces a metaphorical interpretation. The author extends this reasoning to the word 'fa' (then), saying it is even more likely to be used metaphorically for 'waw'. He then applies this to another divorce case using 'fa', stating that the ikhtilaf (difference) among scholars parallels the difference over 'waw', but since literal meaning is preferred, consensus is chosen. The passage concludes with multiple hadith versions confirming the ruling on expiation when a better option is seen, supporting the metaphorical interpretation.

Key Terms
ثم (thumma): A particle meaning 'then', used here metaphorically as 'waw' (and) when literal meaning is impossible.
واو العطف (waw al-'atf): The conjunction 'and' used for coordination; here it replaces the literal meaning of 'thumma'.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical or non-literal usage of a word; applied to particles when literal meaning is legally impossible.
حقيقة (haqiqa): Literal meaning; the default interpretation unless impossibility forces majaz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the literal meaning is impossible to implement since expiation before the act is not obligatory.
B) Because the majority of narrations use 'waw' so 'thumma' is a scribal error.
C) Because Abu Hanifa preferred metaphorical interpretation in all cases.
D) Because the word 'thumma' always means 'waw' in legal contexts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that when literal meaning is legally impossible, metaphorical interpretation becomes necessary is demonstrated through the interpretation of 'thumma' as 'waw' in the oath expiation hadith.

Hatta as Ghayah: Linguistic Limit and Legal Consequence
Analysis of 'hatta' as limit and legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the particle hatta as indicating ghayah (limit) versus purpose (li-kay) and apply this distinction to determine the breaking of an oath in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
The legal force of 'hatta' hinges on whether it marks a limit or a purpose—only the former triggers an oath break upon cessation.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul unpacks the linguistic and legal dimensions of the particle 'hatta'. Distinguishing between its function as a ghayah (limit) and as a marker of purpose (li-kay) is essential for deriving correct rulings in oaths and other areas. By mastering the grammatical signs—nasb for extension, raf for a new clause—you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion to real cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary uses of hatta
From the passage, note that hatta can indicate a ghayah (limit) or a purpose (li-kay). The grammatical sign for ghayah is nasb (subjunctive) on the following verb; for purpose, it is also nasb but the semantic context differs.
Also note the rare case of pure conjunction (raf' with a new sentence).
2
Apply the Hanafi criterion for ghayah
The passage states: the initial action must admit extension, and the clause after hatta must be suitable as an indication of termination. If both hold, it is a ghayah; if not, consider purpose or conjunction.
E.g., 'beat until you scream' – beating admits extension, screaming is a termination indicator.
3
Analyze the Quranic examples
Examine verses like 'until they give jizyah' (ghayah with nasb) and 'until there is no fitnah' (purpose). Observe how the criterion determines the meaning.
The same particle can have different legal implications based on context.
4
Apply to the oath case from al-Ziyadat
In the oath example, 'until you scream' is a ghayah. Therefore, stopping before that point breaches the oath. Contrast with 'until you feed me dinner', which is a supplication (purpose), so stopping does not breach.
The ruling depends entirely on the nature of hatta in the condition.

وقُرىء : حَتَّى يَقُولُ الرَّسُولُ : بالرفع ، على معنى جملة مبتدأة: أي كا حتى الرسول يقول ذلك، فلا يكون فعلهم سبباً له، ويكون متناهياً به.

ولو قال: عبدي حرِّ إن لم آتكَ حتى تُغديني، فأتاه فلم يُغده: لم يحنث ؛ لأن قوله : حتى تغديني : لا يصلح دليلاً على الانتهاء، بل هو داع
And it is a ghayah (limit), so it was a deficient reality. And upon this: I ate the fish until its head—with nasb—meaning I ate it as well. And it may enter upon a nominal sentence like the waw of conjunction when used for joining sentences, and it is a ghayah. If the khabar of the mubtada' is mentioned, then it is its khabar; otherwise, it must be established from something similar to what preceded. You say: I beat the people until Zayd was angry—this is a nominal sentence, and it is a ghayah. And from that: I ate the fish until its head—with raf', except that the khabar is not mentioned here, so it must be established from something similar to what preceded, with the possibility that it is attributed to him or to someone else, meaning: until its head is my eating or someone else's eating. And its places in verbs are to be made as a ghayah meaning 'to', or as a ghayah of a nominal sentence. And the sign of ghayah is that the first part admits extension, and the last part is suitable as an indication of termination. If it is not sound, then it is for consequence in the sense of lam: 'kay'—this if the first part is a cause and the last is not suitable as a ghayah but is suitable as a consequence. And this is similar to the section of conjunction of nouns; if this is impossible, it is made metaphorical for pure conjunction, and the meaning of ghayah becomes invalid. Upon this are the issues of our companions in 'al-Ziyadat'. And for this sentence, apart from pure metaphor, there is mention in the Book of Allah Almighty. Allah Almighty said: 'Until they give the jizyah willingly while they are humbled' (Tawbah 29). And He said: 'Until you bathe' (Nisa 43)—it is in the meaning of 'to'. And likewise: 'Until you ask permission' (Nur 27), and many similar. And He said: 'And fight them until there is no fitnah' (Baqarah 193)—meaning 'so that'. And He said: 'And they were shaken until the Messenger said' (Baqarah 214)—with nasb in two ways: one: 'until the Messenger would say', so their action is not a cause for the Messenger's statement; at his statement, as is the nature of limits, they indicate termination, and their action ends without effect. The second: 'and they were shaken so that the Messenger would say', so their action is the cause for his statement, and this does not necessitate termination. And it was recited: 'until the Messenger says' with raf', meaning a nominal sentence: that is, 'until the Messenger says that', so their action is not a cause for it, and it is terminated by it. And Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said in al-Ziyadat: concerning a man who said to a man: 'My slave is free if I do not beat you until you scream, or until you complain of my hand, or until so-and-so intercedes, or until the night enters'—these are ghayat. So if he stops before the ghayat, he breaks his oath because the action, by way of repetition, admits extension in the context of fulfillment, and stopping it is necessitated in the context of breaking the oath, unavoidably. And these matters are indications of stopping the beating, so acting upon their reality is obligatory, so the condition for breaking becomes stopping before the ghayah. And if he said: 'My slave is free if I do not come to you until you feed me dinner', and he came but did not feed him, he does not break his oath because his words 'until you feed me dinner' are not suitable as evidence of termination; rather, they are a supplication.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul explains the linguistic and legal function of the Arabic particle 'hatta'. The author begins by stating that 'hatta' can act as a ghayah (limit/goal), but when used as such, its reality is 'deficient' in the sense that it requires completion. He illustrates the grammatical distinction: when 'hatta' takes nasb (subjunctive) after a verb, it indicates that the action extends up to that point (e.g., 'I ate the fish until its head' meaning the head was also eaten). When it takes raf' (nominative), it introduces a new clause and the implied predicate (khabar) must be supplied from context, e.g., 'until its head [was eaten]'. He then provides a criterion: a true ghayah must allow the first part to extend and the last part to indicate termination. If not, 'hatta' may mean 'so that' (li-kay) expressing purpose, which does not indicate a limit. He notes that the Hanafi scholars applied these distinctions in their rulings, as found in al-Ziyadat by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. The passage then cites Quranic verses to show the different uses: 'until they give jizyah' (ghayah with nasb), 'until you bathe' (ghayah meaning 'to'), 'until there is no fitnah' (purpose), and 'until the messenger said' (two interpretations). Finally, he presents fiqh rulings: if someone swears an oath using 'hatta' to set a condition, the breaking occurs if he stops before that condition is met, because 'hatta' indicates a limit. But if the phrase does not serve as a true limit (e.g., 'until you feed me'), stopping does not break the oath. This demonstrates how usul al-fiqh analyzes linguistic tools to derive correct legal outcomes.

Key Terms
غاية (ghāyah): A limit or goal that terminates an action; the state after which the action ceases.
حتى (ḥattā): A particle that can indicate a ghayah (limit), purpose (li-kay), or mere conjunction depending on context.
جملة مبتدأة (jumlat mubtada'ah): A nominal sentence that is independent and not a continuation of the preceding verb; with 'hatta' it introduces a new clause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears 'My slave is free if I do not beat you until you scream,' and he stops beating before the person screams, what is the ruling?
A) He does not break his oath because 'hatta' here indicates purpose.
B) He breaks his oath because 'hatta' indicates a limit, and stopping before that limit breaks the oath.
C) The oath is invalid because the condition is impossible.
D) He must complete the beating to avoid breaking the oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, identifying whether 'hatta' indicates a limit (ghayah) or purpose determines the legal consequences in oaths and other rulings.

The Primary Meaning of 'Ba': Attachment (al-Ilsaq)
Defining the primary literal meaning of 'ba'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary meaning of the preposition 'ba' as attachment (al-ilsaq) and apply this understanding to interpret Hanafi legal examples from sales and oaths.

Core Idea
Ba primarily means attachment, affecting contract structure and oath scope.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational Hanafi usul principle that the Arabic preposition 'ba' (ب) carries a core literal meaning of attachment (الإلصاق). This is not arbitrary; it is established by Arab usage and has profound implications for how contracts and oaths are interpreted. By understanding this single linguistic feature, you will be able to analyze why a sale phrased with 'ba' differs legally from one without, and why an oath using 'ba' excludes falsehood while one using 'an' includes it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The text states that 'ba' is for attachment (الإلصاق). Recognize that this is claimed to be its primary (haqiqi) meaning.
2
Analyze the first legal example
In the sale sentence 'I bought this slave for a kurr of wheat', the 'ba' attaches the price (kurr) to the object (slave), making it a valid price. Contrast with the reversed sentence where the contract is added to the kurr, turning it into a salam sale.
3
Apply to the oath example
In 'If you inform me of the arrival of Zaid' (with 'ba'), the 'ba' indicates that the object of informing is omitted; only truthful news is considered. Compare with the 'an' version that includes lying.
4
Note the incomplete passage
The source text cuts off while discussing the 'an' construction; we work with the available portion.
5
Summarize the legal effect
Ba attaches the following noun to the verb, making it a condition or follower, which changes the legal classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حروف الجر
أما الباء فللإلصاق هو معناه بدلالة استعمال العرب، وليكون له معنى يخصه هو له حقيقة.
ولهذا صَحِبَتِ الباء الأثمانَ فيمن قال: اشتريت منك هذا العبد بكر من حنطة، ووَصَفَها أن الكُرَّ ثمن يصح الاستبدال به.
بخلاف ما إذا أضاف العقد إلى الكرِّ، فقال: اشتريتُ منك كرَّ حنطة، ووَصَفَها بهذا العبد أنه يصير سلماً، لا يصح إلا مؤجلاً، ولا يصح الاستبدال به؛ لأنه إذا أضاف البيعَ إلى العبد: فقد جعله أصلاً، وألصقه بالكر، فصار الكرُّ شرطاً يُلصَق به الأصل.
ور وهذا حد الأثمان التي هي شروط وأتباع، ولذلك قلنا: في قول الرجل: إن أخبرتني بقدوم فلان فعبدي حر: إنه يقع على الحقِّ؛ لأن ما صَحِبَه الباء: لا يصلح مفعول الخبر، ولكنَّ مفعول الخبر محذوف؛ بدلالة حرف الإلصاق.
كما تقول: بسم الله: أي بدأتُ به، فيكون معناه: إن أخبرتني خبراً مُلصقاً بقدومه، والقدوم اسم لفعل معلوم موجود.
بخلاف قوله: إن أخبرتني أن فلاناً قد قدم: فإنه يتناول الكذب أيضاً؛ لأنه غير مشغول بالباء، فصلح مفعولاً، وأنَّ مع ما بعدها مصدر، ومعناه:
Chapter of Prepositions. As for 'ba', it is for attachment; that is its meaning by the indication of Arab usage, and so that it may have a meaning specific to it, which is its literal meaning. For this reason, 'ba' accompanies prices when one says: 'I bought this slave from you for a kurr of wheat,' and he describes that the kurr is a price with which substitution is valid. Unlike when he adds the contract to the kurr, saying: 'I bought from you a kurr of wheat' and describes it with this slave, it becomes a salam sale, not valid except deferred, and substitution with it is not valid; because when he adds the sale to the slave, he has made it the principal object and attached it to the kurr, so the kurr becomes a condition attached to the principal. And this is the definition of prices that are conditions and followers. Therefore we said: In a man's statement: 'If you inform me of the arrival of Zaid, then my slave is free' — it applies to truth; because what is accompanied by 'ba' cannot be the object of the verb of informing, but the object of the verb is omitted by indication of the particle of attachment. Just as you say: 'Bismillah' meaning 'I begin with it', so its meaning is: 'You informed me of a news attached to his arrival', and 'arrival' is a name for a known existing action. Unlike his statement: 'If you inform me that Zaid has arrived' — it includes lying as well; because it is not occupied by 'ba', so it is valid as an object, and 'an' with what follows is a verbal noun, and its meaning is: (incomplete).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on prepositions (حروف الجر), specifically the letter 'ba'. The author defines its primary literal meaning as 'attachment' (الإلصاق), based on how Arabs use it. He then demonstrates this through legal examples. In a sale, when one says 'I bought this slave for a kurr of wheat', the 'ba' attaches the price (kurr) to the object (slave), making the price a valid substitute. But if one reverses the sentence to 'I bought a kurr of wheat for this slave', it becomes a salam sale (advance payment for deferred delivery), which has different rules because the structure changes the roles. This distinction shows that 'ba' fixes the attached item as a condition or follower. Similarly, in an oath: 'If you inform me of Zaid's arrival (biqudum)', the 'ba' indicates that the object of 'inform' is omitted; only the truth matters because the 'ba' attaches the news to the arrival. But if the oath uses 'an' (that), it includes falsehood as well because no attachment is indicated. The passage cuts off, but it illustrates the legal implications of this linguistic feature.

Key Terms
الباء (al-ba): Preposition indicating attachment (الإلصاق).
الإلصاق (al-ilsaq): Attachment; the core meaning of the letter 'ba'.
ثمن (thaman): Price; when attached by 'ba', it becomes a valid substitute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Assistance (المعاونة)

Key Takeaway
The letter 'ba' in legal texts signifies attachment, determining whether an item is a price, a condition, or the object of an oath.

The Primary Meaning of 'Min' in Hanafi Usul
Defining the primary meaning of 'min' as tab'eed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the primary meaning of the preposition min (tab'eed) and apply it to interpret legal texts where min appears.

Core Idea
Min in Hanafi usul primarily indicates partiality (tab'eed) — it picks out a part, not the whole.

Prepositions like min carry technical meanings in usul al-fiqh that affect legal interpretation. This passage from Al-Bazdawi defines min's primary sense as tab'eed (partiality). Understanding this is essential for correctly interpreting statements in contracts, oaths, and other legal contexts where min appears.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term under discussion
Locate the term 'min' in the source text. Note that the author is defining its primary meaning for legal purposes.
The text begins with 'وأما من' (As for min).
2
State the primary meaning
Extract the meaning given: 'فللتبعيض' (it is for partiality). Understand that this is the original meaning (أصلها) for which it was placed.
The author emphasizes that this is the core sense, not a secondary usage.
3
Apply the meaning to the example
Consider the example 'أعتق من عبيدي' (free from my slaves). Apply the partitive sense: the command requires freeing some slaves, not all.
This shows how the legal obligation changes depending on the presence of min.
4
Recognize the range of application
The author notes that issues involving min are numerous. Reflect on how this partitive meaning affects various fiqh topics, such as wills, bequests, and oaths.
This step prepares you for more complex derivations.

The Letters of Oath: Ba, Waw, and Ta
Explaining the use of ba, waw, and ta in oaths and their metaphorical extensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three letters of oath (ba, waw, ta) and explain the metaphorical derivation of waw and ta from ba according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Ba is the original oath letter; waw and ta are metaphorical extensions that expand the language of oaths.

In Hanafi usul, the words used in oaths have specific linguistic properties that affect legal rulings. This passage examines the letters ba, waw, and ta, showing how waw and ta derive from ba through metaphor and phonetic similarity. Understanding these relationships helps you grasp why certain oath phrases are valid and others are not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original letter and its properties
Locate the passage's claim about ba: it is the original letter for oaths, indicating adhesion (ilṣāq). Note its use with an implied verb and its extension to pronouns and attributes.
Ba is not exclusive to oaths; it can be used generally for adhesion.
2
Trace the metaphor from ba to waw
The passage states waw is used metaphorically for ba due to phonetic similarity (both articulated by pursing lips) and semantic similarity (conjunction resembles adhesion). Identify these two grounds.
The metaphor allows waw to substitute for ba but restricts the verb from being explicit.
3
Understand the restriction on revealing the verb with waw
Why does the passage say one cannot say 'aḥlifu wallāh'? Because waw is a metaphor for ba, and revealing the verb would make the metaphor general, losing its specificity to oaths.
This restriction is a key practical consequence: 'Wallah' is valid but 'aḥlifu wallāh' is not.
4
Analyze the further metaphor from waw to ta
The passage explains that ta is used metaphorically for waw because both are extra letters (ḥurūf zawā'id) in Arabic. The need for expansion in oaths justifies this extra step.
The example 'turaath' for 'wiraath' shows the pattern of ta substituting waw in other contexts.
5
Synthesize the overall linguistic principle
The passage illustrates a Hanafi principle: linguistic expansions (taʿaddī al-ṣilāt) through metaphor are permissible in oaths to accommodate strong need, with the condition that the metaphor remains specific to the domain.
This principle affects validity of oath formulations in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حروف القسم : ومن ذلك حروف القسم: وهي الباء، والواو، والتاء، وما وُضع وهو : أيم الله ، وما يؤدي معناه ، : لَعَمْرُ الله. فأما الباء : فهي التي للإلصاق، وهي دالة على فعل محذوف معناه : أقسم بالله، أو : أحلف بالله. وكذلك في سائر الأسماء والصفات، وكذلك في الكنايات. تقول : بك لأفعلن كذا، و به لأفعلن كذا ، فلم يكن لها اختصاص بالقسم. وأما الواو : فإنها استعيرت لمعنى الباء؛ لأنها تناسبه صورةً ومعنى، أما الصورة: فإن صورتها وجودها في مخرجها بضم الشفتين، مثل الباء، وأما المعنى : فإن عَطْفَ الشيء على غيره: نظير إلصاقه به، فاستُعير له، إلا أنه لا يحسن إظهار الفعل ها هنا، تقول: والله، ولا تقول: أحلف والله ؛ لأنه استُعير للباء؛ توسعةً لصلات القسم. ولو صح الإظهار لصار مستعاراً بمعنى الإلصاق، فتصير الاستعارة عامة في بابها، وإنما الغرض بها الخصوص لباب القسم الذي يدعو إلى التوسعة، ويُشبه قَسَمَيْن. ولا يدخل في الكناية : أعني الكاف، ثم استعير التاء بمعنى: الواو ؛ توسعة لشدة الحاجة إلى القسم ؛ لما بين الواو والتاء من المناسبة، فإنهما من حروف الزوائد في كلام العرب. مثل التراث : فإنه لغةٌ في الوراث، والتورية في الوراة، وما أشبه ذلك. ولما صار ذلك دخيلاً على ما ليس بأصل: انحطت رتبته عن رتبة (۱) من ورى الزَّنْد، يري ورياً : أي أخرج ناره. وكشف الأسرار ١٨٥/٢ . سائد.
Letters of oath. And from that are the letters of oath: they are ba, waw, ta, and what is placed: 'aym Allah' and what conveys its meaning: 'la'amr Allah'. As for ba: it is for adhesion, and it indicates an omitted verb meaning: I swear by Allah, or I take an oath by Allah. And similarly for all names and attributes, and similarly for pronouns. You say: 'By you, I will do such and such' and 'By him, I will do such and such', so it does not have exclusivity to oaths. As for waw: it is used metaphorically for the meaning of ba because it resembles it in form and meaning. As for form: its form is that it is articulated by pursing the lips, like ba. As for meaning: the conjunction of one thing to another is similar to its adhesion to it, so it is used metaphorically for it, except that it is not good to reveal the verb here. You say: 'W-Allah', and you do not say: 'I swear W-Allah' because it is used metaphorically for ba; to expand the connections of oath. And if revealing were valid, it would become metaphorical in the meaning of adhesion, so the metaphor would become general in its domain, but the purpose is specificity to the domain of oath which calls for expansion, and it resembles two oaths. And it does not enter into pronouns: I mean the kaf, then ta was used metaphorically in the meaning of waw; to expand due to the strong need for oath, because of the similarity between waw and ta, for they are both from the extra letters in Arabic speech. Like 'turaath' which is a dialectal variant of 'wiraath', and 'tawriya' is a variant of 'wawra', and the like. And when that became intrusive on what is not original, its rank decreased from the rank of... from 'wara al-zand' meaning he brought out its fire. Kashf al-Asrar 2/185.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage explains the classification and usage of oath letters in Arabic, focusing on the Hanafi linguistic approach. The letters of oath are ba, waw, ta, and also the set phrases 'aym Allah' and 'la'amr Allah'. Ba is the primary letter indicating adhesion; it is used with an implied verb 'aqsimu' or 'ah lifu'. Waw is used metaphorically for ba because they share articulation (lip rounding) and semantic relation (adhesion/conjunction). However, the verb cannot be expressed with waw; one says 'Wallah' not 'ahlifu Wallah'. This restriction shows the metaphor is specific to oaths. Ta is further derived from waw due to similarity as extra letters; it is used for expansion, like 'turaath' (inheritance) instead of 'wiraath'. The passage reflects Hanafi attention to linguistic patterns as a basis for legal rulings on oaths.

Key Terms
قسم (qasam): Oath; a solemn declaration invoking Allah
باء (ba): The letter ba used for oaths, indicating adhesion
واو (waw): The letter waw used metaphorically for ba in oaths
تاء (ta): The letter ta used metaphorically for waw in oaths, as an extra letter
استعارة (istiʿāra): Metaphor; the use of a word in a non-literal sense based on resemblance
حروف الزوائد (ḥurūf al-zawā'id): Extra letters; letters that can be added to words without altering the root meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of oath letters shows that ba is literal, waw is a metaphor for ba, and ta is a metaphor for waw, all serving to expand the language of oaths while maintaining legal precision.

Oath Particles (Harf al-Qasam) in Hanafi Usul
Linguistic analysis of oath particles in Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the original and borrowed oath particles (ba, waw, ta) in Arabic, explain the Hanafi restrictions on using waw and ta, and apply the linguistic reasoning (isti'ara) to evaluate the validity of oath formulations.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, ba is the original oath particle; waw and ta are borrowed with specific restrictions, and their hierarchy determines valid oaths.

When we swear an oath in Arabic, we use particles like ba, waw, or ta. But these are not interchangeable. The Hanafi school, following Basran grammarians, provides a detailed linguistic analysis of these particles, examining their origins, borrowing (isti'ara), and restrictions. In this lesson, we dissect a passage from al-Bazdawi's usul work to understand the hierarchy and its implications for valid oath formulations in Hanafi practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original oath particle
Begin by identifying that ba (ب) is the original particle used with any noun (e.g., billahi, bika). It is not specific to oaths and can be used with an explicit verb (aqsimu billahi).
Ba is flexible and unrestricted in oaths.
2
Understand the borrowing (isti'ara) of waw
Waw (و) is borrowed from ba due to resemblance in articulation (bilabial) and meaning (attachment). However, waw cannot be used with an explicit verb; you say 'wallahi' but not 'ahlifu wallahi'.
The borrowing is restricted to the oath context without the verb to preserve specificity.
3
Understand the borrowing of ta
Ta (ت) is borrowed from waw because both are extra letters (huruf al-zawa'id). Ta is further restricted: it is only used with the name 'Allah' (e.g., tallahi), not with other divine names like al-Rahman.
Ta's rank is lower than waw and ba, hence stricter limitation.
4
Analyze the deletion of the oath particle
When the oath particle is omitted for brevity (e.g., 'Allahu la-af'alanna'), there is a grammatical dispute. The Hanafi school (following Basrans) holds the word is accusative (Allaha) because the genitive-governing particle is gone. Kufans hold genitive (Allahi) by contextual understanding.
The Hanafi position is 'wa huwa madhhabuna' (it is our school).
5
Examine special oath forms: aym Allah and la-'amr Allah
For 'aym Allah', the Kufans derive it from 'ayman' (plural of yamin), but Hanafis consider it an independent particle (silat) with no derivation, analogous to particles like 'sah'. For 'la-'amr Allah', the lam is for initiation and 'amr' means existence, making the meaning explicit 'by the existence of Allah', analogous to explicit sale contract language.
These forms are valid oaths but analyzed distinctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسم بالله، أو : أحلف بالله. وكذلك في سائر الأسماء والصفات، وكذلك في الكنايات. تقول : بك لأفعلن كذا، و به لأفعلن كذا ، فلم يكن لها اختصاص بالقسم. وأما الواو : فإنها استعيرت لمعنى الباء؛ لأنها تناسبه صورةً ومعنى، أما الصورة: فإن صورتها وجودها في مخرجها بضم الشفتين، مثل الباء، وأما المعنى : فإن عَطْفَ الشيء على غيره: نظير إلصاقه به، فاستُعير له، إلا أنه لا يحسن إظهار الفعل ها هنا، تقول: والله، ولا تقول: أحلف والله ؛ لأنه استُعير للباء؛ توسعةً لصلات القسم. ولو صح الإظهار لصار مستعاراً بمعنى الإلصاق، فتصير الاستعارة عامة في بابها، وإنما الغرض بها الخصوص لباب القسم الذي يدعو إلى التوسعة، ويُشبه قَسَمَيْن. ولا يدخل في الكناية : أعني الكاف، ثم استعير التاء بمعنى: الواو ؛ توسعة لشدة الحاجة إلى القسم ؛ لما بين الواو والتاء من المناسبة، فإنهما من حروف الزوائد في كلام العرب. مثل التراث : فإنه لغةٌ في الوراث، والتورية) في الووراة، وما أشبه ذلك. ولما صار ذلك دخيلاً على ما ليس بأصل: انحطت رتبته عن رتبة الأول والثاني، فقيل : لا تدخل إلا في اسم الله تعالى؛ لأنه هو المقسم به غالباً، فجاز تالله ، ولم يَجُزْ: تالرحمن، وتالرحيم. وقد يُحذف حرفُ القَسَم تخفيفاً، فيقال: الله لأفعلن كذا. لكنه بالنصب عند أهل البصرة، وهو مذهبنا، وبالخفض: عند أهل الكوفة. وقد ذكر في الجامع ما يتصل بهذا الأصل، مثل قول الرجل : والله الله، والله الرحمن والرحيم على ما ذكرنا في «الجامع». وأما : أيم الله : فأصله : أيمن الله ، وهو جَمْعُ: يمين، وهذا مذهب أهل الكوفة. وأما مذهب أهل البصرة، وهو قولنا أن ذلك صلة وضعت للقسم، لا اشتقاق لها. مثل : صَهْ، ومَهُ، وبَحْ، والهمزة للوصل، ألا يُرى أنها توصل إذا تقدمها حرفٌ مثل سائر حروف الوصل، ولو كان لبناء الجمع وصيغته : لَمَا ذهب عند الوصل، والكلام فيه يطول. وأما : لعَمْرُ الله : فإن اللام فيه للابتداء، والعمر: هو البقاء، ومعناه: لبقاء الله، هو الذي أُقسم به، فيصير تصريحاً لمعنى القسم، بمنزلة قول الرجل : جعلتُ هذا العبد ملكاً لك بألف درهم : أنه تصريح لمعنى البيع، فيجري مجراه ، فكذلك هذا.
I swear by Allah, or: I take an oath by Allah. And similarly for all other names and attributes, and similarly for metonymies. You say: 'By you I will do such-and-such', and 'By him I will do such-and-such', so it has no specificity to oaths. As for the waw: it is borrowed for the meaning of ba because it resembles it in form and meaning. As for form: its form is its existence in its point of articulation with the lips together, like the ba. As for meaning: the joining of one thing to another is similar to adhering it to it, so it was borrowed for it, except that it is not appropriate to mention the verb here. You say: 'Wallah', and you do not say: 'Ahlifu wallah' because it is borrowed for the ba to expand the connections of oaths. If mentioning the verb were valid, it would become borrowed in the sense of adhesion, making the borrowing general in its category, while the purpose is specific to the category of oaths which call for expansion, and it resembles two oaths. It does not enter into metonymy: I mean the kaf. Then the ta is borrowed in the meaning of waw to expand due to the great need for oaths because of the suitability between waw and ta, for they are both extra letters in Arabic speech, like 'al-turath' (inheritance) which is a dialect for 'al-wurath', and 'al-tawriyah' (the Torah) for 'al-wawrah', and similar. When this became a substitute for what is not original, its rank fell below the first and second, so it was said: It does not enter except into the name of Allah Almighty because it is the one by whom oaths are mostly made, so 'tallahi' is allowed, and 'tal-rahman' and 'tal-rahim' are not allowed. Sometimes the oath particle is omitted for brevity, and one says: 'Allahu la-af'alanna kadha'. But it is in the accusative according to the Basrans, and that is our school, and in the genitive according to the Kufans. And it has been mentioned in 'al-Jami' what is connected to this principle, such as the statement of a man: 'Wallahi Allah, wallahi al-rahman wa al-rahim' as we mentioned in 'al-Jami'. As for 'aym Allah': its origin is 'ayman Allah', and it is the plural of 'yamin', and this is the view of the Kufans. As for the view of the Basrans, and it is our statement, that it is a particle placed for the oath with no derivation. Like 'sah', 'mah', 'bah', and the hamza for connection; do you not see that it is connected when a letter precedes it like other connectors? And if it were for the form of the plural, it would not disappear when connected, and the discussion about it is long. As for 'la-'amr Allah': the lam is for initiation, and 'al-amr' is existence, and its meaning is 'by the existence of Allah', which is what is sworn by, so it becomes an explicit expression for the meaning of the oath, like the statement of a man: 'I made this slave your property for a thousand dirhams' as an explicit expression for the meaning of sale, so it runs its course. So likewise this.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi usul analysis of the particles used in oaths (qasam). The author begins by stating that the particle ba (ب) is the original oath particle, used with any name or attribute, and it is not specific to oaths (e.g., 'bika la-af'alanna' meaning 'by you I will do'). Then he discusses waw (و) which is borrowed (musta'ar) from ba due to similarity in articulation (both bilabial) and meaning (attachment). However, unlike ba, one cannot explicitly mention the verb 'ahlifu' with waw; one must say 'Wallahi' not 'Ahlifu Wallahi'. This restriction preserves the specificity of waw to oaths. Next, ta (ت) is borrowed from waw because both are extra letters (huruf al-zawa'id) in Arabic, but ta is even more restricted: it is only used with the name 'Allah' (e.g., 'Talahi') and not with other names like 'Rahman' or 'Rahim'. This is because ta's rank is lower than waw and ba. The passage then addresses the deletion of the oath particle for brevity, as in 'Allahu la-af'alanna'. The Basran grammarians, whose view is adopted by the Hanafi school, hold that the word 'Allah' is in the accusative case (nasb) because the particle that would govern the genitive is gone. The Kufans hold it remains genitive (khafd). Finally, the author discusses two special oath forms: 'aym Allah' and 'la-'amr Allah'. For 'aym Allah', the Kufans derive it from 'ayman' (plural of yamin, oath), while the Basrans (and Hanafis) consider it a particle (silat) specifically devised for oaths with no derivation, analogous to other particles like 'sah' and 'mah'. For 'la-'amr Allah', the lam is for emphasis and 'amr' means existence; the phrase means 'by the existence of Allah', which is an explicit expression of the oath's meaning, similar to explicitly stating 'I hereby sell' in a contract.

Key Terms
قسم (qasam): An oath; the act of swearing by Allah or other entities.
باء (ba): The original oath particle, used with any noun, e.g., 'billahi'.
واو (waw): A borrowed oath particle (from ba), used only with the verb omitted, e.g., 'wallahi'.
تاء (ta): A borrowed oath particle (from waw), used only with the name 'Allah', e.g., 'tallahi'.
كناية (kinayah): Metonymy; in oaths, words that imply swearing without explicit particles (like 'ya' or 'kaf').
إستعارة (isti'ara): Linguistic borrowing (metaphorical transfer) where a word is used in a non-primary sense due to resemblance.
أيم الله (aym Allah): An oath form; Hanafi view: it is a particle (silat) specifically placed for oaths, not derived from 'yamin'.
لعمر الله (la-'amr Allah): An oath meaning 'by the life of Allah', where 'amr' is existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Al-ba (ب)
B) Al-waw (و)
C) Al-ta (ت)
D) Al-kaf (ك)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hierarchy of oath particles (ba original, waw borrowed with verb omission, ta restricted to 'Allah') is based on linguistic analysis (isti'ara) and determines the validity of oath formulations.

The Particle متى in Conditional Divorce: Temporal Ambiguity and Immediate Fulfillment
Explaining the legal effect of the ambiguous particles متى, من, ما in divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the usul characteristics of the particle متى and apply them to determine the ruling in conditional divorce statements.

Core Idea
متى is a noun for vague time, making it conditional like إن, but its temporal reality triggers immediate fulfillment of a condition that refers to the present.

This passage explores how the ambiguous particle متى functions in conditional divorce. Unlike إن which is purely conditional, متى carries a temporal element—it denotes vague time. This nuance affects the ruling: when a husband says 'You are divorced when I do not divorce you,' the condition is instantly true because he is not divorcing her at that moment. We will also see how من and ما follow similar logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of متى
Analyze the definition given in the passage: 'متى فاسم للوقت المبهم' (a noun for vague time). In Hanafi usul, this means it shares the ambiguity of إن but retains its temporal reality.
The ambiguity allows it to function as a conditional particle.
2
Apply the conditional logic to a present-state condition
Consider the phrase 'أنت طالق متى لم أطلقك'. The condition 'لم أطلقك' (I do not divorce you) is a present state. At the moment of utterance, he has not divorced her, so the condition is immediately true.
Thus divorce occurs at the moment of speech.
3
Distinguish from a future-oriented condition
Contrast with 'متى شئت' (when you wish). Here the condition is her future wish, not yet fulfilled. The divorce is suspended until she wishes.
متى does not always produce immediate divorce; it depends on the condition's temporal reference.
4
Generalize to من and ما
Recognize that من and ما are also ambiguous and enter the same chapter. Many legal issues arise, especially with من (which refers to persons).
Their ambiguity makes them conditional, but the specific rulings depend on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما : متى فاسمٌ للوقت المبهم بلا اختصاص، فكان مشاركاً لـ: إن في الإبهام، فلزم في باب المجازاة. وجُزم بها، مثل: إن لكن مع قيام الوقت ؛ لأن ذلك حقيقتها، فوقع الطلاق بقوله : أنتِ طالق متى لم أطلقك : عقيب اليمين. :وقوله : متى شئت : لم يقتصر على المجلس. وكذلك : متى ما . * وقد سبق تفسير : كلَّما. وكذلك مَن و ما يدخلان في هذا الباب؛ لإبهامهما، والمسائل فيهما كثيرة، خصوصاً في مَن
As for 'mata', it is a noun for vague time without specificity, so it participates with 'in' in ambiguity, so it is necessary in the chapter of conditional sentences. It is jussive, like 'in', but with the establishment of time because that is its reality, so divorce occurs by his saying: 'You are divorced when I do not divorce you' immediately after the oath. And his saying: 'when you wish' is not restricted to the session. And similarly 'mata ma'. And the interpretation of 'kullama' preceded. And similarly 'man' and 'ma' enter this chapter due to their ambiguity, and the issues concerning them are many, especially regarding 'man'.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage discusses the particles متى (mata), من (man), and ما (ma) in conditional divorce. متى is a noun for vague time; like إن, it creates a condition, but because its primary meaning is time, it has specific rulings. For example, if a husband says 'you are divorced when I do not divorce you' (أنت طالق متى لم أطلقك), the condition is immediately fulfilled since he has not divorced her, so divorce occurs right away. The phrase 'متى شئت' (when you wish) is not restricted to the sitting—the wife can choose anytime. Similarly, متى ما (mata ma) follows the same rule. The particle كلما (kullama 'every time') was explained previously. Likewise, من (man) and ما (ma) enter this chapter because of their ambiguity, and they have many legal issues, especially من which can refer to a person.

Key Terms
متى (mata): A noun for vague time; used in conditional sentences like إن but with a temporal element.
مَن (man): An ambiguous particle meaning 'who' or 'whoever'; used in conditions.
ما (ma): An ambiguous particle meaning 'what' or 'whatever'; used in conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Divorce does not occur because of ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
متى is a time-based conditional particle; if the condition describes a present state, divorce is immediate due to its temporal reality.

The Particles 'لو' and 'لولا' in Conditional Divorce
Ruling on the conditional particles 'لو' and 'لولا' in divorce based on narration from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the particles 'لو' and 'لولا' in conditional divorce, apply the Hanafi ruling from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and explain the linguistic basis for the difference.

Core Idea
'لو' suspends divorce on a future condition; 'لولا' denies it because the condition is already present.

In this lesson, we examine a narration from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the effect of the conditional particles 'لو' and 'لولا' in divorce pronouncements. Understanding the subtle linguistic difference is essential for correctly applying Hanafi rulings on suspended divorces. The passage shows that 'لو' creates a valid condition (like 'إن'), while 'لولا' does not effect divorce because it implies refusal of an existing state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particles
Locate the two particles 'لو' and 'لولا' in the text and note their literal meanings.
2
Contextualize the conditional
Understand that these particles are used in a conditional divorce statement. The husband's utterance is the basis for the ruling.
3
Analyze 'لو'
Note that 'لو' is equated to 'إن' because it contains the meaning of anticipation (tarabbus). Thus, it functions as a conditional particle suspending divorce on a future event.
The operative phrase: 'لأن فيها معنى الترقب'.
4
Analyze 'لولا'
Recognize that 'لولا' has a conditional meaning but leads to non-occurrence of divorce. Because it implies the condition is already present or impossible, it does not create a valid future condition.
The operative phrase: 'لما فيه من معنى الشرط'—but the conditional meaning prevents occurrence.
5
Apply to examples
Practice with given and new examples to solidify the distinction.
E.g., 'أنت طالق لو دخلت الدار' vs 'أنت طالق لولا صحبتك'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد روي عن أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : فيمن قال: أنت طالق لو دخلت الدار : إنه بمنزلة قوله : إن دخلت الدار ؛ لأن فيها معنى الترقب فعملت عمل الشرط. وكذلك قول الرجل : أنت طالق لولا صحبتك، وما أشبه ذلك : غيرُ واقع ؛ لما فيه من معنى الشرط.
And it has been narrated from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, regarding one who says: 'You are divorced if I enter the house' that it is like his saying: 'If you enter the house', because it contains the meaning of anticipation, so it acts as a condition. And similarly, a man's saying: 'You are divorced if not for your companionship' and similar things: it does not occur, because it contains a conditional meaning.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the particles 'لو' (law) and 'لولا' (lawla). When a husband says 'you are divorced if I enter the house' (أنت طالق لو دخلت الدار), it is treated exactly like saying 'if you enter the house' (إن دخلت الدار) because 'لو' contains the meaning of anticipation (tarabbus) and thus functions as a condition. Consequently, divorce is suspended on the condition. On the other hand, when a husband says 'you are divorced if not for your companionship' (أنت طالق لولا صحبتك) or similar expressions, the divorce does not occur because 'لولا' has a conditional meaning but implies refusal of a condition that is present, making the condition impossible or irrelevant. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for determining whether a conditional divorce takes effect.

Key Terms
لو (law): A particle meaning 'if' used for hypothetical conditions; in this context, it acts like a conditional particle for future events.
لولا (lawla): A particle meaning 'if not' or 'were it not for'; it has a conditional meaning but prevents the occurrence of the conditional act because the condition is already present or impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, what is the ruling if a man says to his wife 'أنت طالق لولا صحبتك'?
A) Divorce occurs immediately.
B) Divorce occurs only if the wife leaves his companionship.
C) Divorce does not occur because 'لولا' has a conditional meaning.
D) Divorce occurs after a waiting period.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'لو' functions as a conditional particle like 'إن', suspending divorce on a future event, while 'لولا' does not effect divorce because it implies that the condition is already present or impossible.

The Particle 'كيف' in Manumission and Divorce
Ruling on the word 'كيف' in declaring manumission and divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the legal effect of the word 'كيف' in declarations of manumission and divorce according to Abu Hanifa and explain the underlying linguistic principle.

Core Idea
The word 'كيف' in manumission effects immediate freedom; in divorce, only one talaq occurs.

This lesson explores the usul al-fiqh analysis of the word 'كيف' (how/ however). In Hanafi law, the same word can have different legal consequences depending on the context—manumission vs. divorce. Abu Hanifa's ruling illustrates how linguistic classification of a term influences its legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Classify 'كيف' linguistically
Recognize that 'كيف' is a question about the state (hal) and is a noun for state. This classification affects its legal interpretation.
2
Apply the principle to manumission
When a master says 'أنت حر كيف شئت', the word 'كيف' does not create a condition but rather describes the state of freedom. Therefore, the freeing is immediate (إيقاع).
Contrast with conditional freeing.
3
Apply the same analysis to divorce
In divorce, saying 'أنت طالق كيف شئت' results in only one divorce (الواحدة). The surplus words 'كيف شئت' are considered surplus in description and quantity, not increasing the number of divorces.
The word 'كيف' refers to manner, not number.
4
Understand the underlying usul principle
The Hanafi approach is based on qiyas jaliy: the word 'كيف' is consistent in its meaning as a state description, so it cannot change the count of divorces. In manumission, it simply confirms the state of freedom.
This shows how linguistic analysis directly impacts legal rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك : كيف: وهو سؤال عن الحال، وهو اسم للحال، فإن استقام، وإلا : بطل. ولذلك قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في قول الرجل : أنت حر كيف شئت: إنه إيقاع. وفي الطلاق: إنه تقع الواحدة، ويبقى الفضل في الوصف والقدر،
That: 'kayfa': it is a question about the state, and it is a noun for state, so if it is consistent, otherwise it is invalid. And for that reason, Abu Hanifa may Allah have mercy on him said regarding a man's saying: 'You are free however you wish': it is an actual freeing. And in divorce: it occurs once, and the surplus remains in description and quantity.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage discusses the word 'كيف' (kayfa, meaning 'how' or 'however'). It is a question about the state (hal) and is a noun for state. When used in declarations of manumission, such as 'you are free however you wish' (أنت حر كيف شئت), it constitutes an effective immediate freeing according to Abu Hanifa. However, in the context of divorce, if a husband says 'you are divorced however you wish' (أنت طالق كيف شئت), only one divorce occurs, and the additional descriptions and quantities (e.g., 'however', 'whenever') remain as surplus that may affect the details? The phrase 'ويبقى الفضل في الوصف والقدر' suggests that the extra words like 'however you wish' are considered surplus and do not increase the number of divorces but may relate to description and quantity. This is based on the principle that 'كيف' refers to the manner, not the number.

Key Terms
كيف (kayfa): A word meaning 'how'; used as a noun for state, questioning the manner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
