Explicit vs. Implicit Speech in Qadhf: The Usul Principle
Clarifying that hadd requires explicit accusation, not implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between sarih and kinaya to determine whether a statement incurs hadd in qadhf, and identify the usul principle that governs this ruling.

Core Idea
Only an explicit accusation triggers hadd; implicit statements are repelled by doubts.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the classification of speech into explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinaya) carries significant legal consequences, especially in penal law. This lesson explores how this distinction operates in the context of qadhf (false accusation of zina), where the hadd punishment is only applied to direct and unambiguous statements. By parsing the text from al-Bazdawi, you will learn to identify the key features of sarih and kinaya, understand why kinaya is considered deficient, and apply the maxim that hudud are repelled by doubts.

Guided Path
1
Classify the Types of Speech
Identify the terms 'sarih' and 'kinaya' in the text. Note that sarih is described as the basis of speech, while kinaya has a deficiency: it requires intention (niyya) to clarify its meaning.
The text states that kinaya is 'like necessities,' meaning it is resorted to only when sarih cannot be used.
2
Connect to the Hudud Principle
The text says that the difference between sarih and kinaya appears 'in what is repelled by doubts' (fi ma yudra'u bi al-shubuhat). This refers to the well-known maxim that hadd punishments are averted by doubts. Because kinaya inherently involves ambiguity, it creates doubt and thus cannot trigger hadd.
Applied: if a statement can be interpreted innocently, the doubt prevents punishment.
3
Apply to the Given Examples
Analyze each example: (a) Someone says 'you have spoken truthfully' (tasdiq) after an explicit accusation – no hadd. (b) 'As for me, I am not a fornicator' intending to imply the other – no hadd. (c) 'It is as you said' – yes, hadd, because it is a direct confirmation that matches the explicit accusation.
Distinguish between 'tasdiq' (general affirmation) and explicit restatement. The latter is tantamount to sarih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفةٌ للمرأة، فإذا زال الإبهام بالنية : كان دلالة على الصريح، لا عاملاً بموجبه.
والأصل في الكلام هو الصريح، وأما الكناية : ففيها ضَرْبُ قُصورٍ من حيث إنه يقصر عن البيان إلا بالنية والبيانُ بالكلام هو المراد، فظهر هذا التفاوت فيما يُدرأ بالشبهات، وصار جنس الكنايات بمنزلة الضرورات. ولهذا قلنا: إن حد القذف لا يجب إلا بصريح الزنا، حتى إن من
قذف رجلاً بالزنا، فقال له :آخر : صدقت: لم يُحد المصدق وكذلك إذا قال: أما أنا فلستُ بزان، يريد التعريض بالمخاطب : لم
yُحد.
وكذلك في كل تعريض ؛ لما قلنا.
بخلاف من قذف رجلاً بالزنا فقال له :آخرُ : هو كما قلت: حُدَّ هذا
الرجل، وكان بمنزلة الصريح ؛ لِمَا عُرف في كتاب الحدود، والله أعلم.
A description of the woman. When the ambiguity is removed by intention, it becomes indicative of the explicit, not operative by its implication. The basis in speech is the explicit. As for implicit speech, it has a type of deficiency in that it falls short of clarification except by intention, and clarification by speech is what is intended. So this difference appears in what is repelled by doubts, and the whole category of implicit speech is like necessities. For this we said: The hadd of qadhf is not obligatory except by explicit accusation of zina. So if a person accuses a man of zina, and another says: 'You have spoken truthfully,' the one who affirmed is not punished. Likewise, if he says: 'As for me, I am not a fornicator,' intending to imply against the addressee, he is not punished. And similarly in every allusion, for what we said. Contrary to the case where someone accuses a man of zina, and another says: 'It is as you said,' that man is punished with hadd, and it is like the explicit, as is known in the Book of Hudud. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage addresses the usul al-fiqh distinction between explicit and implicit speech (sarih and kinaya) and its application in the law of qadhf. The author states that speech is fundamentally explicit. Implicit speech is deficient because it does not clarify without intention. Therefore, in rulings that are repelled by doubts (like hudud), implicit speech does not incur the hadd. The author provides examples: if A accuses B of zina and C says 'you have spoken truthfully,' C is not punished because his statement is implicit. Similarly, if someone says 'I am not a fornicator' intending to imply the other, it is not punishable. However, if C says 'it is as you said,' it is explicit and incurs hadd. This illustrates that the hadd of qadhf requires a direct and explicit accusation of zina, not merely an implication.

Key Terms
الصريح (al-sarih): The explicit statement that does not require intention to clarify its meaning; it is the basis of speech.
الكناية (al-kinaya): Implicit speech that falls short of clarity without accompanying intention; it is compared to necessities.
التعريض (al-ta'rid): Allusion or indirect statement; like kinaya, it does not incur hadd in qadhf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 'As for me, I am not a fornicator' intending to imply the other.
C) 'It is as you said' after an explicit accusation.
D) Any allusion (ta'rid) to zina.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the hadd of qadhf requires an explicit (sarih) accusation; implicit and allusive statements are not punishable due to the principle that hudud are repelled by doubts.

8 min
Effect of Condition on Obligation: Hanafi vs. Opponent
Rational argument on condition as interposer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a condition (shart) blocks the cause (sabab) from connecting to its locus (mahall), preventing obligation until the condition is fulfilled, and evaluate the opposing argument's distinction between financial and physical obligations.

Core Idea
A condition interposes between cause and locus; obligation does not arise until the condition is met.

This passage delves into a fundamental usul al-fiqh dispute: when a condition is attached to a cause (sabab mu'allaq), does it merely delay the obligation or does it prevent the cause from producing any obligation until the condition occurs? The author presents an opposing view that distinguishes financial from physical obligations, then provides the Hanafi counter-argument using analogies from sales and oaths. Understanding this debate clarifies how conditions modify the causal chain in deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal issue
What is the question being debated? Restate it in your own words. Look for the point of disagreement: whether a condition delays or prevents the obligation.
The issue: Does ta'liq (suspension by condition) negate the immediate effect of a cause or merely postpone performance?
2
Paraphrase the opponent's claim and its justification
The opponent holds that the condition only defers performance, not the obligation. Their proof is the distinction between financial and physical obligations: financial permits separation of obligation and performance; physical does not, so delay negates the obligation.
The opponent concedes that for physical acts, delay eliminates the obligation, but insists that for financial acts, the obligation remains despite the delay.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The author argues that the command (ijab) must be attached to its locus (mahall); the condition interposes, so the cause never connects. Analogies: part of a sale does not obligate; a sale of a free person is invalid; an oath on righteousness does not directly cause expiation.
Key principle: A cause must be a path (tariq) leading to its effect; a suspended cause is like an oath on righteousness—it points away from expiation, not toward it.
4
Extract the underlying usul principle
The Hanafi position rests on the necessity of connection (ittisal) between cause and locus. Without that connection, the cause is ineffective.
This principle applies uniformly to all types of obligations, rejecting the opponent's financial-physical distinction.
5
Apply the principle to a concrete example
If a husband says, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' the divorce (obligation to separate) does not take effect until entry. The condition blocks the cause (pronouncement) from connecting to its locus (the wife) until entry occurs.
The practical consequence: no obligation exists prior to entry; it is not a delayed obligation but a contingent one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : لأن الوجوب يثبت بالإيجاب لولا الشرط، فيصير الشرط مُعدماً ما وجب وجوده لولا هو فيكون الشرط مؤخراً، لا مانعاً. ولا يلزم أن تعجيل البدني في الكفارات لا يجوز على قوله؛ لأن الوجوب بالسبب حاصل، ووجوب الأداء متراخ بالشرط، والمالي يحتمل الفصل بين وجوبه وبين وجوب أدائه وأما البدني فلا يحتمل الفصل بين وجوبه وبين وجوب أدائه، فلما تأخر الأداء: لم يبق الوجوب. ولنا : أن الإيجاب لا يوجد إلا بركنه ولا يثبت إلا في محله، كشطر البيع لا يوجب شيئاً، وبيع الحرِّ باطل أيضاً، وها هنا الشرط حال بينه وبين المحل ، فبقي غير مضاف إليه، وبدون الاتصال بالمحل : لا ينعقد سبباً. ألا يُرى أن السبب ما يكون طريقاً، والسبب المعلق يمينُ عُقدت على البِرِّ، والعقدُ على البِرِّ ليس بطريق إلى الكفارة؛ لأنه لا يجب إلا بالحنث، وهو نقض العقد، فكان بينهما تناف، فلا يصلح سبباً.
He said: Because obligation is established by the command were it not for the condition, so the condition nullifies what would have been obligated without it, making the condition a deferrer, not a preventer. And it does not follow that immediate [performance] of a physical act in expiations is not allowed according to his statement; because the obligation due to the cause is actual, and the obligation of performance is deferred by the condition, and financial [obligations] allow a separation between their obligation and the obligation of their performance, whereas physical [obligations] do not allow separation between their obligation and the obligation of their performance, so when performance is delayed, the obligation does not remain. And our [argument]: that the command does not exist except with its pillar and is not established except in its locus, like part of a sale does not obligate anything, and the sale of a free person is also invalid, and here the condition interposes between it and the locus, so it remains not attached to it, and without connection to the locus it does not become a cause. Do you not see that the cause is a path, and a suspended cause is an oath sworn on righteousness, and a contract on righteousness is not a path to expiation because expiation is only obligated by breaking the oath, which is a nullification of the contract, so there is contradiction between them, so it is not suitable as a cause.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is an argument (istidlal) about the effect of a condition (shart) on an obligation (wujub). The author, likely summarizing a debate, begins by stating the opposing view: that a condition merely delays performance but does not negate the underlying obligation. The opponent distinguishes between financial (mali) and physical (badani) obligations: for financial, the delay is permissible, but for physical, the obligation ceases if performance is delayed. The author then provides a counter-argument using the analogy of a sale: a command (ijab) requires its pillar (rukn) and must be attached to its locus (mahall). A condition interposes between the command and its locus, preventing the cause (sabab) from being effective. Further, the author uses the example of an oath: if someone swears to do good, the oath is not directly a cause for expiation (kaffarah); expiation only arises from breaking the oath (hanth). Thus, a suspended cause is contradictory and cannot be a valid cause. The legal principle is that a condition on a cause blocks its connection to the effect, so the obligation is not actualized until the condition is met.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition that suspends the effect of a cause; it interposes between the cause and its locus.
إيجاب (ijab): The act of imposing an obligation; the command that creates a legal duty.
سبب (sabab): The cause that leads to a legal ruling; a path to the effect. Must be connected to its locus.
محل (mahall): The subject or locus upon which the ruling operates (e.g., the slave for manumission, the wife for divorce).
بدني (badani): Physical acts, such as fasting, which cannot be performed in parts and require immediate performance upon obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the condition interposes between the cause and its locus, preventing connection
C) Because the obligation is only for financial matters, not physical ones
D) Because the cause must be immediate and cannot be delayed

Key Takeaway
A condition on a cause prevents the cause from connecting to its locus; no obligation arises until the condition is fulfilled.

Condition vs Term and the Effect on Suspended Oaths: Zufar's Challenge
Clarification of shart vs ajal, with a refutation of Zufar's view on suspended oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a condition (shart) and a term (ajal) in Hanafi usul, and apply this distinction to evaluate whether a suspended oath persists when the subject (mahall) ceases to exist.

Core Idea
A condition enters the cause itself; an option enters the ruling — the difference determines when an oath is broken.

In Hanafi legal theory, not all delays are created equal. A contract may be delayed by a term (ajal) that does not affect its cause, or by a condition (shart) that temporarily blocks the cause from connecting to its locus. Misunderstanding this distinction leads to errors in analyzing suspended oaths, conditional divorces, and sales with options. This passage from the usul text sharpens that distinction and addresses a challenge from Imam Zufar regarding the persistence of oaths after the subject disappears.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish condition from term
Identify that a condition (shart) enters upon the cause (sabab) itself, preventing it from connecting to its locus (mahall). A term (ajal) only delays performance without affecting the cause.
The author uses the analogy of a conditional manumission ('You are free if...') to show that the condition blocks the cause from taking effect.
2
Understand the sale with option analogy
In a sale with khiyar al-shart, the option enters on the ruling (bindingness), not the cause, because the sale itself does not bear risk. This contrast highlights that a condition operates at the cause level.
The author emphasizes that sale with option does not break an oath not to sell because the cause (sale) is complete, but the oath is about the act of selling.
3
Apply to oaths
Analyze the examples: if one swears not to sell and sells with an option, he breaks the oath; if one swears not to divorce and then suspends divorce (e.g., 'If you enter, you are divorced'), he does not break the oath because the condition attaches to the cause, making the divorce non-immediate.
The ruling difference arises because the condition in the divorce suspends the cause, whereas the option in sale does not suspend the cause.
4
Analyze Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that since the suspended command (ijab) is invalid (does not create an immediate effect), the existence of the locus is not required for the oath to persist. Thus, if one swears by triple divorce and then actually divorces thrice before the condition occurs, the oath remains.
Zufar's reasoning relies on the uncertainty of the condition's occurrence; he gives precedence to the current state of ownership.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The author counters using the example of suspending divorce upon a future marriage (e.g., 'If I marry her, she is divorced'). Such a suspension is valid even though the marriage does not currently exist, proving that the condition does not require current existence of the locus. Therefore, Zufar's requirement for continued existence is unnecessary.
The author holds that the oath is nullified when the subject disappears; Zufar's view that the oath remains is rejected by the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتبيَّن أن الشرط ليس بمعنى الأجل ؛ لأن هذا داخل على السبب الموجب، فمَنَعَه عن اتصاله بمحله فصار كقوله : أنت: متى لم يتصل بقوله : حرِّ : لم يعمل، فصار الحكم معدوماً بعد الشرط بالعدم الأصلي، كما كان قبل اليمين. - وهذا بخلاف البيع بخيار الشرط؛ لأن الخيار ثمة دخل على الحكم، دون السبب حقيقةً، وحكماً: أما الحقيقة فلأن البيع لا يحتمل الخطر، وإنما يُثبت الخيار، بخلاف القياس نظراً، فلو دخل على السبب لتعلّق حكمه لا محالة، ولو دخل على الحكم : لنَزَل سبه ، وهو مما يحتمل الفسخ، فيصلح التدارك به، بأن يصير غير لازم بأدنى الخطرين فكان أولى، وأما هذا فيحتمل الخطر، فوجب القولُ بكمال التعليق في هذا الباب. وأما الحكم فإن من حلف لا يبيع فباع بشرط الخيار : حنث. ولو حلف لا يطلق، فحلف بالطلاق: لم يحنث. وإذا بطلت العُلْقة : صار ذلك الإيجاب علةً، كأنه ابتداء، ولهذا صح تعليق الطلاق والعتاق قبل الملك به. ولهذا لم يجز تعجيل النذر المعلّق، وتعجيل الكفارة، وهو كالكفارة بالصوم. وفَرْقُه : باطل؛ لأنا قد بينا أن حق الله في المال: فعل الأداء، لا عين المال، إنما يُقصد عين المال في حقوق العباد، وأما في حقوق الله تعالى: فلا فإنما العبادة فعل لا مال وإنما المال آلتُه. 6 وقال زفر رحمه الله : ولما بطل الإيجاب: لم يُشترط قيام المحل لبقائه، فإذا حلف بالطلاق الثلاث، ثم طلقها ثلاثاً: لم تبطل اليمين، وكذلك العتق. وإنما شرط قيام الملك ؛ لأن حال وجود الشرط متردد، فوجب الترجيح بالحال، فإذا وقع الترجيح بالملك في الحال: صار زوال الحل في المستقبل، من حيث إنه لا ينافي وجوده عند وجود الشرط لا محالة، وزوال الملك في المستقبل سواء. ألا يُرى أن التعليق بالنكاح يجوز وإن كان الحل للحال معدوماً، فلو كان التعليق يتصل بالمحل : لما صح تعليق الطلاق في حق المطلقة ثلاثاً بنكاحها.
And it is clarified that the condition is not in the sense of a term; because this [condition] enters upon the obligating cause, preventing it from connecting to its locus, so it becomes like his statement: 'You are free if...' when it does not connect to his statement 'free', it does not take effect, so the ruling becomes non-existent after the condition by original non-existence, as it was before the oath. And this is unlike a sale with option; because there the option enters upon the ruling, not the cause, in reality and in ruling: regarding reality, because a sale does not bear risk, but the option is established, contrary to analogy and opinion; so if it entered upon the cause, its ruling would necessarily attach, and if it entered upon the ruling, its cause would descend, and it is something that bears rescission, so remediation by it is suitable, such that it becomes non-binding with the lesser of two risks, so it is preferable. And as for this [condition], it bears risk, so complete suspension (ta'liq) must be said in this chapter. As for the ruling: if someone swore not to sell, and then sold with an option, he breaks his oath. If he swore not to divorce, and then divorced with an oath [as a condition], he does not break his oath. And when the connection is invalidated, that command becomes a cause ('illah), as if it were a new beginning, and for this reason suspending divorce and manumission before ownership is valid. And for this reason, it is not permitted to expedite a suspended vow or expedite expiation, and it is like expiation by fasting. And his distinction is invalid; because we have explained that the right of Allah in wealth is the act of performance, not the wealth itself; wealth is intended in the rights of people, but in the rights of Allah it is not; worship is an act, not wealth, and wealth is merely its instrument. 6 And Zufar, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Since the command (ijab) is invalid, the existence of the locus for its continuation is not required; so if he swore by triple divorce and then divorced her three times, the oath is not nullified, and similarly for manumission. And the existence of ownership is only required because the state at the time of the condition is uncertain, so preference by the current state is necessary; so when preference is made by current ownership, the dissolution of marital permission in the future becomes [effective], since it does not contradict its existence at the time of the condition definitely, and the future loss of ownership is equal. Do you not see that suspension upon marriage is valid even though marital permission is currently non-existent? So if the suspension connected to the locus, then suspending divorce in the case of a woman divorced three times upon her [future] marriage would not be valid.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage expands on the distinction between a condition (shart) and a term (ajal). A condition enters upon the cause (sabab) itself, preventing it from attaching to its locus (mahall), so the effect is as if non-existent from the start. In contrast, a sale with an option (khiyar al-shart) involves the option entering upon the ruling (hukm), not the cause, because the sale itself cannot bear risk; the option allows rescission and affects the binding nature of the contract. The author then applies this: if someone swears not to sell and then sells with an option, he breaks his oath because the sale is complete in its cause; but if he swears not to divorce and then divorces by giving his wife the option (using a suspended pronouncement), he does not break the oath because the divorce is not immediate. Further, the author discusses suspended vows (nadhr) and expiations (kaffarah): they cannot be expedited because the obligation is not yet due. He refutes an opponent's distinction between financial and physical obligations regarding Allah's rights: the right of Allah in financial matters is the act of performance (like paying zakat), not the money itself. Finally, Zufar's opinion is presented: since the suspended command is invalid (because it does not create an immediate effect), the existence of the locus at the time of the condition is not required for the persistence of the oath. Thus, if someone swears using triple divorce ('If I do X, then my wife is divorced three times'), and then actually divorces her three times before the condition, the oath is not nullified because the suspension is independent of the locus. The author critiques this by noting that suspension upon marriage (like 'If I marry her, she is divorced') is valid even though the daughter is not currently a wife, so the condition does not require current existence of the locus.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): A fixed term or deadline for performance; differs from a condition in that a term does not affect the cause but only the timing.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option in a contract that allows rescission within a specified period; it enters upon the ruling (bindingness) of the contract, not the cause.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a ruling upon a future condition; it is complete (kamil) in certain areas like divorce and manumission.
نذر معلق (nadhr mu'allaq): A conditional vow that becomes binding upon fulfillment of the condition; it cannot be expedited before the condition.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for broken oaths or other violations; it is not due until the cause (hanth) occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between a condition (shart) in a suspended cause and an option (khiyar) in a sale?
A) A condition enters on the ruling, while an option enters on the cause.
B) A condition enters on the cause, while an option enters on the ruling.
C) Both enter on the cause in the same way.
D) A condition is like an option in that both affect the timing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a condition (shart) prevents the cause from taking effect until fulfilled, while a term (ajal) only delays performance; this distinction is critical in analyzing suspended oaths and contracts.

Takhyir (Choice) Between Obligations in Hanafi Usul
Analysis of takhyir in obligations with examples from slave's Friday prayer, vow of fasting, and mudabbar slave analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi usul principle of takhyir in obligations and apply it to distinguish cases where alternatives are equal vs. different in legal nature.

Core Idea
Takhyir allows choice only when the alternatives are similar in legal essence; if they differ, the original obligation stands.

In this passage, the author unpacks the concept of takhyir (choice) when an obligation can be fulfilled in multiple ways. He provides concrete examples—ranging from a slave's attendance at Friday prayer to a vow of fasting—and clarifies when the choice is permissible and when it is not. The discussion also reveals an important intra-madhhab difference between Zahir al-Riwayah and Nawadir, and contrasts the Hanafi approach with that of al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario of takhyir
Analyze the first example: a master permits his slave to attend Friday prayer. Identify the two options available to the slave and the ruling that he may choose.
The two options: 4 rak'ahs (Zuhr) or 2 rak'ahs (Jumu'ah).
2
Understand the reasoning for choice
Determine why the slave is given a choice. Note that the permission defaults to Jumu'ah, but since the two prayers differ in legal nature (daily obligation vs. weekly replacement), seeking ease (rifq) is appropriate.
The text says: 'because Friday is the original when permission is given, and because they are different, so seeking ease is appropriate.'
3
Examine the vow case and the two transmissions
Look at the second example: a vow to fast for a year upon entering a house. Compare the ruling in Nawadir (choice to fast or expiate) versus Zahir al-Riwayah (obligation to fulfill the vow). Understand why they differ: in Nawadir, the two acts are considered equal? Actually, the text says in Nawadir the choice is allowed because the vow and kaffarah are different? Wait, the text says in Zahir al-Riwayah fulfillment is obligatory because they differ in meaning. Clarify: the author contrasts the two transmissions.
In Nawadir, Abu Hanifa allows expiation; in Zahir al-Riwayah, the original vow must be fulfilled.
4
Apply the mudabbar slave analogy
Understand the analogy: just as the master of a mudabbar slave pays the lesser of two compensations, when two obligations are equal (like the slave's prayer options), choice is given.
The text says: 'In our case, they are equal, so it becomes like the mudabbar slave.'
5
Evaluate the Musa example
Consider the example of Musa (AS) being given the choice between eight or ten years of service. Recognize that the eight years were the obligatory dowry, and the extra two years were a voluntary favor—so the choice was about an optional extra, not an equivalent obligation.
This reinforces that takhyir only applies when options are legally equal.

Source Text

لما قلنا وهو الذي وعدناه في أول هذا الفصل.
وإنما تمسك الشافعي رحمه الله في هذا الباب بظاهر العزيمة والرخصة، كما هو دأبه في دَرْك حدود الفقه، والله أعلم.
أربعاً، هي
ولا يلزم على ما قلنا رجل أذن لعبده في الجمعة : أنه إن شاء صلى وهو الظهر، وإن شاء صلى ركعتين وهي الجمعة؛ لأن الجمعة الأصل عند الإذن، ولأنهما مختلفان، فاستقام طلب الرفق.
وكذلك مَن قال : إن دخلتُ الدارَ فعليَّ صيامُ سَنَةٍ، ففعل وهو معسر : كان له أن يصوم سنةً، أو يكفِّرَ بصيام ثلاثة أيام عند محمد رحمه الله.
وهو مروي في «النوادر» عن أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه. أما في ظاهر الرواية : فيجب الوفاء به، لا محالة؛ لأن ذلك مختلف في المعنى، أحدهما: قُربةٌ مقصودة، والثاني: كفارة.
وفي مسألتنا : هما سواء، فصار كالمدبر إذا جنى: لزم مولاه الأقل من الأرش، ولا يلزم أن موسى عليه السلام كان مخيَّراً بين أن يرعى غَنَمَ شعيب عليه السلام ثماني حِجَج، أو عشراً، فيما ضمن من المهر؛ لأن الثمانية كانت مهراً لازماً، والفضل كان براً منه، والله أعلم.
ويتصل بهذه الجملة معرفة حكم الأمر والنهي في ضد ما نُسبا إليه، وهذا تابع غير مقصود في جنس الأحكام؛ فأخرناه.
As we said, and that is what we promised at the beginning of this chapter. Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, adhered in this chapter to the apparent meaning of 'azimah and rukhsah, as is his custom in grasping the boundaries of fiqh. And Allah knows best. Four? [unclear] It does not follow from what we said that if a man permits his slave to attend Friday prayer, then he may pray if he wishes four rak'ahs (Zuhr) or pray two rak'ahs (Friday); because Friday is the original when permission is given, and because they are different, so seeking ease is appropriate. Similarly, whoever says: 'If I enter the house, then I must fast for a year,' and then enters while insolvent, then he may either fast for a year or expiate by fasting three days according to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him. And this is narrated in al-Nawadir from Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him. As for Zahir al-Riwayah, fulfillment is obligatory without fail, because they differ in meaning: one is an intended act of worship, and the other is an expiation. In our case, they are equal, so it becomes like the mudabbar slave when he commits a crime: his master is liable for the lesser of the two values (the compensation). And it is not necessary that Musa, peace be upon him, was given a choice between tending the sheep of Shu'ayb, peace be upon him, for eight years or ten, regarding what was specified as the dowry; because the eight years were an obligatory dowry, and the extra was a favor from him. And Allah knows best. And connected to this summary is the knowledge of the ruling of command and prohibition regarding their opposites, and this is a subordinate matter not intended in the category of rulings; so we postponed it.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage discusses the principle of takhyir (choice) when an obligation can be fulfilled in multiple ways. The first example: a master permits his slave to attend Friday prayer; the slave may choose between praying Zuhr (4 rak'ahs) or Jumu'ah (2 rak'ahs). The reasoning is that permission defaults to the original obligation (Jumu'ah), but since the two prayers are different in nature (Zuhr is the daily obligation, Jumu'ah is a weekly replacement), the slave is given the easier option. The second example concerns a vow to fast for a year upon entering a house. According to a narration from Abu Hanifa in al-Nawadir, an insolvent person may either fulfill the year-long fast or expiate by fasting three days (kaffarah). However, in Zahir al-Riwayah, the original obligation must be fulfilled because a vowed fast and a kaffarah differ in legal meaning: one is an intended act of worship (qurbah maqsudah), the other is a penal expiation. The author then analogizes to the case of a mudabbar slave who commits a crime: the master pays the lesser of the two compensations. The example of Musa (AS) choosing between eight or ten years of service shows that when a choice is given between a lesser and a greater obligation, the lesser is the default. The passage concludes by linking this discussion to the upcoming chapter on command and prohibition regarding their opposites, which is a subordinate topic postponed for later.

Key Terms
تخيير (takhyīr): The legal option to choose between multiple valid ways of fulfilling an obligation.
ظاهر الرواية (ẓāhir al-riwāyah): The established and authoritative transmission of the school's rulings from Imam Muhammad's six core books.
النوادر (al-nawādir): Rare or non-standard narrations from the founding imams, not part of the six core books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in the case of a man who permits his slave to attend Friday prayer, what does the slave have the option to do?
A) He may only pray Zuhr (4 rak'ahs).
B) He may only pray Jumu'ah (2 rak'ahs).
C) He may choose between praying Zuhr or Jumu'ah.
D) He must pray both Zuhr and Jumu'ah.

Key Takeaway
Takhyir in obligations is permitted only when the alternative ways are equal in legal essence; if they differ, the original obligation must be fulfilled according to the authoritative Zahir al-Riwayah.

The Effect of a Command (Amr) on Its Opposite
Discussion of the effect of a command on its opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four scholarly opinions on whether a command implies a ruling on its opposite, and state the preferred Hanafi view that it implies dislike (karāha).

Core Idea
A command does not automatically prohibit its opposite; it only implies dislike (karāha).

In this passage from Bazdawi's Usul, we examine a key debate: when the Lawgiver commands an act, does that command have any legal effect on the act's opposite? For instance, if standing is commanded, does that make sitting prohibited or disliked? The scholars disagreed, and the Hanafi school settled on a nuanced position. This lesson unpacks the opinions specifically for commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
Understand the issue: whether a command for something (amr bi-shay') has any ruling (hukm) regarding its opposite (ḍidd) when that opposite is not explicitly mentioned.
The opposite is defined as the act that is logically contradictory to the commanded act, e.g., sitting versus standing.
2
List the four opinions on amr
Enumerate: (a) No effect at all, (b) Al-Jassas: necessitates prohibition of opposite(s), (c) Some: necessitates dislike (karāha), (d) Preferred: implies dislike (karāha) (this is the Hanafi view).
The text attributes the second view to al-Jassas and the fourth as 'asahh' (more correct) according to the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the argument of the first group
Their proof: silence (sukūt) is not a valid legal proof; therefore, since the command is silent about the opposite, it cannot establish any ruling.
They reason: if silence is not even a proof for what it directly addresses except through ta'lil (causal reasoning), then it is even less suitable for something else.
4
Understand why the preferred view still holds
The Hanafi view acknowledges that silence is not a direct proof, but through ta'lil one can infer dislike (karāha) without relying on silence as a direct proof. This is a form of rational implication from the command's existence.
The preferred view does not claim a strong legal effect like prohibition, but a weak effect of dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب
حكم الأمر والنهي في أضدادهما
و
اختلف العلماء في الأمر بالشيء: هل له حكم في ضده إذا لم يُقصد
ضده بنهي؟
قال بعضُهم : لا حكم له فيه أصلاً.
وقال الجصاص رحمه الله :
يوجب
ضده إن كان له ضد النهي عن
واحد، أو أضداد كثيرة.
وقال بعضهم : يوجب كراهية ضده.
وقال بعضُهم : يقتضي كراهية ضده، وهذا أصح عندنا.
وأما النهي عن الشيء : فهل له حكم في ضده؟ فعلى هذا أيضاً قال الفريق الأول: لا حكم له فيه بوجه.
وقال الجصاص رحمه الله : إن كان له ضد واحد : كان أمراً به، وإن
كان له أضداد: لم يكن أمراً بشيء منها.
وقال بعضُهم : يوجب أن يكون ضده في معنى سُنَّةِ واجبة. وعلى القول المختار: يَحتمل أن يقتضي ذلك.
احتج الفريق الأول : بأن كل واحدٍ من القسمين ساكت عن غيره، وقد بينا أن السكوت لا يصلح دليلاً شرعياً ، ألا يُرى أنه لا يصلح دليلاً لما
وضع له فيما لم يتناوله إلا بطريق التعليل، فلغير ما وضع له أولى.
Chapter on the Ruling of Command and Prohibition Regarding Their Opposites. The scholars differed concerning the command for something: Does it have a ruling regarding its opposite when its opposite is not intended by a prohibition? Some said: It has no ruling at all regarding it. Al-Jassas, may Allah have mercy on him, said: It necessitates the prohibition of its opposite if it has a single opposite, or many opposites. Some said: It necessitates the dislike (karahah) of its opposite. And some said: It implies the dislike of its opposite, and this is more correct according to us. As for prohibition of something: Does it have a ruling regarding its opposite? Regarding this also, the first group said: It has no ruling whatsoever regarding it. Al-Jassas said: If it has a single opposite, then it is a command for it; and if it has many opposites, then it is not a command for any of them. Some said: It necessitates that its opposite be in the meaning of a wajib sunnah. And according to the chosen view, it is possible that it implies that. The first group argued that each of the two categories is silent about the other, and we have shown that silence is not a valid legal proof. Do you not see that it is not a valid proof for what it was placed for except by way of ta'lil (causal reasoning), so for other than what it was placed for, it is even more unsuitable.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This is a classic usul al-fiqh discussion on whether a command (amr) for an act has any legal effect on its opposite (ḍidd), and similarly whether a prohibition (nahy) has any effect on its opposite. The passage presents four opinions. The first opinion holds that a command has no effect at all on its opposite. The second, attributed to al-Jassas, says that a command necessitates the prohibition of its opposite if there is one opposite; if there are multiple opposites, it necessitates prohibition of all. The third opinion says a command necessitates dislike (karāha) of its opposite. The fourth, which the author states is the preferred (aṣaḥḥ) view according to the Hanafi school, is that a command implies dislike of its opposite, meaning it is makrūh to do the opposite. For prohibition, the opinions mirror: the first group says no effect; al-Jassas says if the opposite is single, the prohibition is a command to do that opposite; if multiple, no command; some say the opposite becomes like a wajib sunnah; and the chosen view is that it may imply that. The first group (the one that denies any effect) supports their position by arguing that each command or prohibition is silent about its opposite, and silence (sukūt) is not a valid legal proof. They reason that if silence is not even a proof for what is directly under discussion except through ta'lil (causal reasoning), then it is even less suitable as evidence for something else. Thus, the correct position is that a command or prohibition does not automatically apply to their opposites; rather, any effect (like dislike or command) must be established by a separate textual indication.

Key Terms
أمر (amr): A demand to perform an action from a superior; in this context, a legal command from the Lawgiver.
نهي (nahy): A demand to abstain from an action; in this context, a legal prohibition from the Lawgiver.
ضد (ḍidd): The opposite or contradictory of the commanded or prohibited act, such as sitting when standing is commanded.
سكوت (sukūt): The absence of any explicit statement about a matter; in legal theory, silence is not a valid source of law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (aṣaḥḥ) in the passage, what is the effect of a command on its opposite?
A) It necessitates the prohibition of the opposite.
B) It has no effect at all.
C) It implies the dislike (karāha) of the opposite.
D) It makes the opposite a wajib sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a command implies dislike (karāha) of its opposite, not prohibition.

The Effect of a Prohibition (Nahy) on Its Opposite
Discussion of the effect of a prohibition on its opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the opinions on the effect of a prohibition (nahy) on its opposite, and explain the Hanafi chosen view that it may imply that the opposite is like a wajib sunnah.

Core Idea
A prohibition may imply that its opposite is like a wajib sunnah, but not a fard.

Just as with commands, scholars debated whether a prohibition (nahy) has a legal effect on its opposite. For example, if the Lawgiver prohibits an act, does that automatically make its opposite commanded or recommended? This lesson examines the symmetrical opinions for prohibitions, culminating in the Hanafi chosen view that the opposite of a prohibited act may be considered like a wajib sunnah—a recommended but not obligatory practice.

Guided Path
1
Understand the symmetrical question
Recognize that the same issue arises for prohibition: does a prohibition (nahy) of something have any ruling on its opposite (ḍidd)? The opinions mirror those for command.
The opposite of a prohibited act is the act of not doing it, or the contrary action if there is a pair.
2
List the opinions on nahy
Enumerate: (a) No effect at all (first group). (b) Al-Jassas: if the opposite is single, it is a command to do it; if multiple, no command. (c) Some: it necessitates that the opposite be in the meaning of a wajib sunnah. (d) Chosen view: it may imply that (i.e., it is possible that the opposite is like a wajib sunnah).
The chosen view is more tentative: 'yahṭamil an yaqtaḍī dhālika' (it may imply that).
3
Examine al-Jassas's view for prohibition
Al-Jassas says: if the prohibited act has only one opposite, then the prohibition is actually a command to do that opposite; if there are multiple opposites, no command is implied.
This is logical: prohibiting one thing leaves its unique opposite as the only permissible alternative, effectively commanding it.
4
Understand the chosen view
The chosen view (al-qawl al-mukhtār) says it is possible that the prohibition implies that its opposite is like a wajib sunnah. This means the opposite is not obligatory (fard) but could be recommended (sunnah wajib in strength).
'Wajib sunnah' is a Hanafi category for an action that is emphasized sunnah (like Eid prayer or witr before it became wajib according to some).
5
Connect with the first group's argument
The first group again argues that silence is not a proof, so no effect. The chosen view accepts that but allows for possibility through implication.
The difference is that the chosen view does not assert certainty, only possibility.

Source Text

The first group (the one that denies any effect) supports their position by arguing that each command or prohibition is silent about its opposite, and silence (sukūt) is not a valid legal proof. They reason that if silence is not even a proof for what is directly under discussion except through ta'lil (causal reasoning), then it is even less suitable as evidence for something else. Thus, the correct position is that a command or prohibition does not automatically apply to their opposites; rather, any effect (like dislike or command) must be established by a separate textual indication.

Key Terms
أمر (amr): A demand to perform an action from a superior; in this context, a legal command from the Lawgiver.
نهي (nahy): A demand to abstain from an action; in this context, a legal prohibition from the Lawgiver.
ضد (ḍidd): The opposite or contradictory of the commanded or prohibited act, such as sitting when standing is commanded.
سكوت (sukūt): The absence of any explicit statement about a matter; in legal theory, silence is not a valid source of law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view (al-qawl al-mukhtār) in the passage, what is the effect of a prohibition on its opposite?
A) It has no effect.
B) It absolutely makes the opposite a wajib sunnah.
C) It may imply that the opposite is like a wajib sunnah.
D) It makes the opposite a fard.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi chosen view is that a prohibition may imply that its opposite is like a wajib sunnah, without certainty.

Definition of the Majhul Narrator and Its Acceptance in Hanafi Usul
Defining the majhūl narrator and his acceptance conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term 'majhūl' in Hanafi usūl, state the two conditions for accepting a majhūl narrator's hadīth, and apply these conditions to evaluate a narrator's status.

Core Idea
A narrator known only through one or two hadiths is not automatically rejected; acceptance hinges on early scholars' attestation or silence.

This lesson defines a specific category of narrator in hadith criticism: the majhūl (unknown) narrator. The Hanafi school takes a nuanced view: obscurity alone does not disqualify. The key is how the early generations (salaf) treated his narrations. If they vouched for him or remained silent, his hadith is accepted. We'll dissect the definition, explore conditions, and see real examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'al-majhūl' in the passage. Note that the author clarifies it specifically means 'unknown in hadith transmission' — known only through one or two hadiths.
Distinguish from other meanings of 'unknown' (e.g., unknown personality).
2
Extract the two acceptance conditions
Read the phrases after 'فإن روى عنه السلف وشهدوا له بصحة الحديث' and 'وإن سكتوا عن الطعن فيه'. The first is explicit testimony; the second is silence implying approval.
Silence here is a sign of acceptance (ridā), not negligence.
3
Analyze the examples provided
The author gives several hadiths from Salama al-Muhabbaq and Ma'qil ibn Sinan. These illustrate that despite being majhūl, their narrations were recorded by major collectors, demonstrating that the condition of salaf approval was met.
Note that the examples are not the proof for the rule but its application.
4
Understand the rationale
The underlying principle is that the salaf were meticulous; if they transmitted and did not criticize, it indicates the narrator was acceptable in their view.
This is a Hanafi position; other schools may differ.
5
Contrast with alternative views
Consider how the Shāfi'ī school generally rejects the majhūl narrator unless corroborated. Recognize that the Hanafi view offers a broader basis for acceptance.
This step prepares for evaluative tasks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المجهول فإنما نعني به المجهول في رواية الحديث، بأن لم يُعرف إلا بحديث، أو حديثين مثل وابصة بن معبد، وسلمة بن المحبق ومعقل بن سنان. فإن روى عنه السلف، وشهدوا له بصحة الحديث: صار حديثه مثل حديث المعروف بشهادة أهل المعرفة. وإن سكتوا عن الطعن فيه بعد النقل فكذلك؛ لأن السكوت يدل على الرضا. وأما سلمة المحبق واسم المحبق صخر، فروى عنه الطبراني عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في رجل وقع على جارية امرأته: إن كان استكرهها فهي حرة، وعليه لسيدتها مثلها، وإن كانت طاوعته فهي له ولها عليه مثلها. رواه النسائي وأبو داود. وأخرج عنه أيضاً أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان في سفر، فأتى على قربة معلقة، فاستقى، فقيل: ميتة، فقال: ذكاة الأديم دباغه، أخرجه النسائي وأبو داود. وأخرج عنه أيضاً أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بعث ببدنتين مع رجل، فقال: أشعرهما من منحرهما. الحديث، ورواه أحمد. وأخرج أيضاً قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: من كانت له حمولة، ويأوي إلى شبع وري: فليصم رمضان حيث أدركه، ورواه أبو داود. وعنه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مر يوم خيبر بقدور فيها لحم حمر الناس، فأمر بها فكفئت. رواه أحمد. وعنه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: البكر بالكر جلد مائة ونفي سنة. رواه أحمد. وحديث: أرأيت لو وجدت مع امرأتك رجلاً. رواه ابن ماجه. وهذا خلاف ما يذكر عنهما. والله ولي الإعانة. وأما معقل بن سنان فروى النسائي عنه أنه قال: مر علي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأنا أحتجم في اثنتي عشرة ليلة خلت من رمضان، فقال: أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم. وعنه: أنه قال لعبد الله بن مسعود: قضى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في بروع بنت واشق امرأة منا مثل ما قضيت. رواه الخمسة وصححه الترمذي.
As for the unknown (majhul): we mean by it the unknown in hadith transmission, i.e., one who is not known except through one or two hadiths, like Wabisa ibn Ma'bad, Salama ibn al-Muhabbaq, and Ma'qil ibn Sinan. If the early generations (salaf) narrated from him and attested to the authenticity of his hadith, his hadith becomes like the hadith of a known narrator by the testimony of knowledgeable people. If they remained silent about criticizing him after transmission, it is likewise [accepted], because silence indicates approval. As for Salama al-Muhabbaq — and al-Muhabbaq's name is Sakhr — al-Tabarani narrated from him from the Prophet, peace be upon him, concerning a man who had intercourse with his wife's slave girl: if he forced her, she becomes free and he owes her mistress her like [in value]; if she consented, she becomes his and he owes her mistress her like. al-Nasa'i and Abu Dawud narrated it. Also from him: that the Prophet, peace be upon him, was on a journey, came upon a water skin hanging, drank, and it was said: it is dead [meat], he said: "The tanning of the skin is its slaughter." al-Nasa'i and Abu Dawud narrated it. Also from him: that the Prophet sent two sacrificial animals with a man and said: "Mark them from their necks." Ahmad narrated it. Also from him: he said: The Messenger of Allah said: "Whoever has a mount and can find sufficiency and drink, let him fast Ramadan wherever he is." Abu Dawud narrated it. Also from him: that the Prophet passed by pots containing the meat of domestic donkeys on the day of Khaybar, and ordered them to be overturned. Ahmad narrated it. Also from him: that the Prophet said: "The virgin with the virgin: one hundred lashes and exile for a year." Ahmad narrated it. Also the hadith: "Have you seen if you found a man with your wife?" Ibn Majah narrated it. This is contrary to what is mentioned about them. And Allah is the Helper. As for Ma'qil ibn Sinan: al-Nasa'i narrated from him that he said: The Messenger of Allah passed by me while I was cupping on the twelfth night of Ramadan, and said: "The cupper and the cupped have broken their fast." Also from him: that he said to 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud: "The Messenger of Allah judged concerning Baru' bint Washiq, a woman from us, just as you judged." The five [major hadith compilers] narrated it, and al-Tirmidhi authenticated it.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'majhul' (unknown) in the context of hadith transmission. The author clarifies that a majhul narrator is one who is not widely known; he is known only through one or two hadiths. Examples given are Wabisa ibn Ma'bad, Salama ibn al-Muhabbaq, and Ma'qil ibn Sinan. The key ruling is that if the early Muslim scholars (the salaf) narrated from such a narrator and testified to the authenticity of his hadith, then his hadith is accepted on par with that of a well-known narrator. Even if they remained silent about him after transmission, it is also accepted because silence in this context indicates approval. The passage then provides extensive hadith examples from Salama and Ma'qil to illustrate that their narrations were indeed accepted by the major hadith collectors. This demonstrates the Hanafi position that a narrator's obscurity is not an automatic disqualification if the early scholars vouch for him or do not criticize him.

Key Terms
المجهول (al-majhūl): A narrator known only through one or two hadiths.
السلف (al-salaf): The early generations of Muslims, especially the Companions and Successors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what makes a narrator 'majhul'?
A) A narrator who is unknown to all scholars.
B) A narrator known only through one or two hadiths.
C) A narrator who is a liar.
D) A narrator who narrates from unknown sources.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a majhul narrator is not automatically disqualified; his hadith is accepted if the early scholars (salaf) either explicitly attest to its authenticity or remain silent after transmission, because silence indicates approval.

Non-Binding Reports and the Dropping of 'Adalah
Classification of reports where 'adalah is dropped

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a report is binding or non-binding and apply the rule that the condition of 'adalah is dropped for non-binding reports, using Hanafi usul examples.

Core Idea
A report that does not impose a binding ruling (ilzam) does not require the reporter to be upright ('adalah).

In Hanafi usul, the condition of 'adalah (uprightness) is not absolute for all reports. This passage explains that when a report is non-binding (ghair mulzim), the need for 'adalah falls away. We will explore the rationale and see how this principle operates across several fiqh examples, from testimony about fosterage to reports by sinful persons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two reasons for dropping 'adalah
The passage opens with two reasons: the general necessity (darura) and the non-binding nature of the report. Focus on the second reason as the main subject of this lesson.
The first reason (darura) is mentioned but not elaborated; the emphasis is on the second.
2
Define the key terms: khabar ghair mulzim and ilzam
A non-binding report is one that does not impose a legal obligation or prohibition on the hearer. Binding reports (mulzim) are those that create a duty or prevent an action.
Examples of binding reports: matters of religion like taharah (purity/impurity of water).
3
Apply the principle to the first set of examples
Analyze why testimony of one person on fosterage (radaa') in marriage is not accepted: it involves imposing rights of others (ilzam haqq al-ibad) so 'adalah is required and a single witness is insufficient.
Contrast with non-binding situations where a single report suffices.
4
Examine the examples from Kitab al-Istihsan
Study the scenarios: a man reports that someone seized his slave and he took it back – accepted. Similarly, a report of repentance and return of property. Also, a report of a sinful person about the slave's permission. All are non-binding (permissive) so 'adalah is dropped.
The pattern: if the report allows something (mujawwiz), it is non-binding.
5
Confirm the principle with the crescent of Ramadan example
Testimony for seeing the crescent is from this category: it does not impose a binding obligation (it only confirms the start of Ramadan, which is already obligatory) so 'adalah is not required.
This shows the breadth of the principle beyond testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : عموم الضرورة الداعية إلى سقوط شرط العدالة. والثاني: أن هذا الخبر غير ملزم، فلم يُشترط شرط الإلزام. بخلاف أمور الدين مثل طهارة الماء، ونجاسته . ولهذا الأصل لم تُقبل شهادة الواحد بالرضاع في النكاح، وفي ملك اليمين وبالحرية؛ لما فيه من إلزام حق العباد. ولهذا لم يُقبل خبرُ الواحد العدلِ في موضع المنازعة؛ لحاجتنا إلى الإلزام، وقَبلْنا في موضع المسالمة. وعلى ذلك بنى محمد رحمه الله المسائل في آخر كتاب الاستحسان، مثل خبر الرجل : إن فلاناً كان غَصَبَ مني هذا العبد، فأخذته منه: يقبل. ولو قال : تاب فردَّه عليَّ قبل خبره ولهذا قبلنا خبر الفاسق في إثبات الإذن للعبد. ولهذا قبلنا خبر المخبر في الرضاع الطارئ على النكاح، أو الموت، أو الطلاق إذا أراد الزوج أن ينكح أختها، أو أرادت المرأةُ نكاح زوج آخر؛ لأنه مجوز: غير ملزم. وأمثلته أكثر من أن تُحصى. والشهادة بهلال رمضان من هذا القسم.
One of them: the generality of the necessity that calls for dropping the condition of uprightness. The second: that this report is not binding, so the condition of bindingness was not stipulated. Contrary to matters of religion such as the purity of water and its impurity. And due to this principle, the testimony of one person on fosterage in marriage is not accepted, and in ownership of the right hand and slavery, because it involves imposing the rights of people. And for this reason, the report of a single upright person is not accepted in a place of dispute because we need bindingness, but we accept it in a place of peace. And upon this, Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) built the issues at the end of the book of al-Istihsan, such as the report of a man: 'So-and-so had seized this slave from me, so I took it from him' – it is accepted. And if he said: 'He repented and returned it to me' – his report is accepted. And for this reason, we accepted the report of a sinful person in establishing permission for a slave. And for this reason, we accepted the report of an informant regarding fosterage that occurs after marriage, or death, or divorce if the husband wants to marry her sister, or if the woman wants to marry another husband, because it is permissive: not binding. And its examples are too many to count. And testimony for the crescent of Ramadan is from this category.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage discusses the second reason for dropping the condition of 'adalah (uprightness) in accepting reports: that the report is not binding (ghair mulzim). It distinguishes this from matters of religion (like tahara) where 'adalah is required because they are binding. The principle is that when a report does not impose a binding ruling (ilzam), the condition of 'adalah is not needed. It gives several Hanafi examples: (1) Testimony of one person on radaa' (fosterage) in marriage is not accepted because it would impose obligations on others (rights of people). (2) A report from a single upright person is not accepted in a dispute (munaza'a) because it would be binding, but is accepted in peaceful situations (musaalama). (3) Imam Muhammad built examples in Kitab al-Istihsan: a man says someone seized his slave and he took it back—his report is accepted because it's permissive; similarly if he says the usurper repented and returned it. (4) The report of a sinful person (fasik) is accepted to prove the permission (idhn) given to a slave. (5) A report about fosterage occurring after marriage, death, or divorce when intending to marry another is accepted because it is permissive, not binding. (6) Testimony for crescent of Ramadan is also from this category. The common thread: all these reports allow something without imposing a binding obligation, so the condition of 'adalah is dropped.

Key Terms
خبر غير ملزم (khabar ghair mulzim): A report that does not impose a binding legal ruling (ilzam) on the listener.
عدالة (adalah): Uprightness; the condition that the reporter be morally and legally upright.
إلزام (ilzam): The quality of imposing a binding obligation or ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the testimony of a single person on fosterage (radaa') in marriage not accepted according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because it is a matter of religion.
B) Because it imposes obligations on the rights of people (ilzam haqq al-ibad).
C) Because the reporter is not upright.
D) Because it is a report of a sinful person.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the condition of 'adalah is dropped for non-binding reports because there is no need to impose a heavy requirement when no obligation follows.

The Permissibility of Tadlis (Kunya) in Narrator Identification
Explains permissibility of concealing narrator's identity to protect from false criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using a kunya for a narrator and identify when it becomes a valid jarh.

Core Idea
Concealing a narrator's name is permissible to protect him, but refusal to disclose upon request makes it criticism.

In hadith criticism, the Hanafi school permits using an indirect reference (kunya) for a narrator to shield him from false accusations. This passage from al-Bazdawi explains the rationale and conditions. You will learn the precise boundary between permissible concealment and condemnable obfuscation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the Arabic terms 'talbis', 'kinayah', and 'jarh' in the passage and understand their definitions in this context.
These terms are central to the discussion; the passage uses them to build the ruling.
2
Understand the ruling
The passage states that using a kunya is not objectionable (la ba'sa bihi) for three reasons: protection from false criticism, protection of the critic, and brevity.
This is a general permissibility, not absolute; conditions follow.
3
Analyze the examples
Two examples are given: Sufyan al-Thawri saying 'Abu Sa'id narrated to me' and Muhammad al-Shaybani saying 'a trustworthy person from our companions narrated to us'.
These illustrate how respected scholars used kunya without blame.
4
Identify the critical condition
The passage explicitly states that using a kunya only becomes a valid jarh (criticism) if the narrator is asked to specify and refuses to explain.
This condition distinguishes permissible concealment from condemnable hiding of weakness.
5
Evaluate the reasoning
The argument is rational (dalil 'aqli): false criticism is unjust, so concealment is justified to protect the innocent; but refusal to clarify when asked implies intentional hiding of a weak narrator.
This aligns with Hanafi preference for preserving narrations over excessive caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و - ومن ذلك طعنهم بالتلبيس على مَن كَنَّى عن الراوي، ولم يُسمه، ولم ينسبه، مثل قول سفيان الثوري: حدثني أبو سعيد، وهو يحتمل الثقة وغير الثقة. ومثل قول محمد بن الحسن رحمه الله: حدثنا الثقة من أصحابنا، من غير تفسير؛ لأن الكناية عن الراوي لا بأس به؛ صيانة له عن الطعن فيه، وصيانة للطاعن واختصاراً. وليس كل من اتُّهِم من وجهِ ما يَسقط به كل حديثه، مثل الكلبي وأمثاله، ومثل سفيان الثوري مع جلالة قدره، وتقدمه في العلم والورع وتسميتُه: ثقة: شهادة بعدالته، فأنى يصير جرحاً؟! ووجه الكناية أن الرجل قد يُطعن فيه بباطل، فيحق صيانته، وقد يروي عمن هو دونه في السن، أو قرينه، أو هو من أصحابه، وذلك صحيح عند أهل الفقه، وعلماء الشريعة. وإن طال سنده: فيكني عنه؛ صيانة عن الطعن بالباطل. وإنما يصير هذا جرحاً: إذا استفسر فلم يُفسِّر.
And from that is their criticism by tadlis (concealing) of one who refers to the narrator by a kunya, without naming him or attributing him, like the statement of Sufyan al-Thawri: 'Abu Sa'id narrated to me,' which could be trustworthy or not. And like the statement of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him: 'A trustworthy person from our companions narrated to us,' without explanation. Because using a kunya for the narrator is not objectionable, as protection of him from being criticized, and protection of the critic, and for brevity. And not everyone who is accused in some respect has all his hadith dropped, like al-Kalbi and his likes, and like Sufyan al-Thawri despite his high rank and precedence in knowledge and piety, and calling him 'trustworthy' is a testimony to his integrity, so how could it be a jarh?! And the reasoning for the kunya is that a man may be falsely criticized, so protecting him is justified, and he may narrate from someone younger than him or his peer or one of his companions, and that is valid according to the people of fiqh and scholars of sharia. And if his chain is long, he uses a kunya as protection against false criticism. And it only becomes a jarh if he is asked to specify and does not explain.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the permissibility of tadlis (concealing the narrator's identity) in hadith transmission. The author argues that referring to a narrator by a kunya (honorific) or general description (e.g., 'a trustworthy person') is allowed to protect the narrator from unjust criticism and to protect the critic from error, as well as for brevity. The author gives two examples: Sufyan al-Thawri saying 'Abu Sa'id narrated to me' (which could refer to reliable or unreliable narrators), and Muhammad al-Shaybani saying 'a trustworthy person from our companions narrated to us' without naming him. Then the author states a key principle: not every accusation against a narrator automatically drops all his hadith. For example, al-Kalbi, despite being accused, is not entirely rejected, and Sufyan al-Thawri, despite his stature, cannot be criticized lightly. The author explains the rationale for using kunya: it protects against false criticism, and it is common to narrate from someone younger or from peers. However, this practice only becomes jarh (valid criticism) if, when questioned, the narrator refuses to identify the person. The passage assumes the Hanafi position that tadlis is not inherently sinful but is contextual.

Key Terms
تلبيس (talbis): Concealment or obscuring the identity of a narrator by using a vague reference
كناية (kināyah): Using an indirect reference (like a kunya) for a narrator to avoid naming them
جرح (jarḥ): A valid criticism that impugns a narrator's reliability and may lead to rejection of their hadith

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the use of a kunya become a valid jarh?
A) When the narrator is young
B) When the narrator is a companion
C) When the narrator refuses to identify the person when asked
D) When the narrator is from the same city

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows concealing a narrator's name for protection unless the narrator refuses to disclose upon request, which then constitutes valid criticism.

Invalid Grounds for Narrator Criticism: The Role of Sin in Jarh
Lists examples of criticisms that are invalid because they are not sinful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that only sinful actions can be grounds for valid jarh, and identify criticisms that are invalid because they target actions that are not sinful.

Core Idea
Criticizing a narrator for truthful joking or horse-riding is invalid jarh — only sins disqualify.

In Hanafi usul, not every criticism of a narrator is valid. To impugn a narrator's reliability, the critic must point to an act that is sinful in sharia. This passage illustrates this principle with three examples: the ignorant criticism of Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, criticism for horse-riding, and criticism for joking. Understanding these examples helps us recognize when a jarh is baseless.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying rule
The passage begins by stating that acts not considered sinful in sharia cannot be grounds for jarh. Extract this rule: ما لا يعد ذنباً لا يصلح جرحاً.
This is the key principle.
2
Analyze the first example: criticism of Imam Muhammad
Consider the criticism that he refused to read hadith. The author argues that even if true, it is not sinful because the manners of a scholar (qudwah) differ from an ascetic. Reject the criticism as invalid.
Personal preference is not a sin.
3
Analyze the second and third examples
Horse-riding is a means of jihad, thus praiseworthy, not sinful. Truthful joking is permitted by sharia. Both are invalid criticisms.
The actions are either virtuous or permissible.
4
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Given a new criticism, ask: Is the criticized action sinful? If not, the jarh is invalid. This is the test derived from the passage.
Always check if the act is a sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك: ما لا يُعد ذنباً في الشريعة مثل طعن الجاهل في محمد ابن الحسن رحمه الله؛ لأنه سأل عبد الله بن المبارك أن يقرأ عليه أحاديث سمعها، فأبى، فقيل له في ذلك، فقال: لا تعجبني أخلاقه. لأن هذا إن صح فليس به بأس؛ لأن أخلاق الفقهاء تخالف أخلاق الزهّاد؛ لأن هؤلاء أهل عُزلة، وأولئك أهل قدوة، وقد يحسن في منزل القدوة: ما يقبح في منزل العزلة، وينعكس ذلك مرةً. وقد قال فيه عبد الله بن المبارك: لا يزال في هذه الأمة مَن يُحيي به دينهم ودنياهم فقيل له: ومَن ذلك اليوم؟ فقال: محمد بن الحسن الكوفي. ومثال ذلك من طعن بركض الدابة مع أن ذلك من أسباب الجهاد، كالسباق بالخيل، والأقدام. ومثل طَعْنِ بعضهم بالمزاح، وهو أمر ورد الشرع به بعد أن يكون حقاً، لا باطلاً، إلا أن يكون أمراً تستفزه الخفة، فيتخبط ولا يبالي.
And from that is what is not considered a sin in sharia, like the ignorant criticism of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, because he asked Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak to read to him hadith he had heard, but he refused, so it was said to him about that, and he said: 'I do not like his manners.' Because if this is true, there is no problem; because the manners of fuqaha differ from the manners of ascetics; because these are people of seclusion, and those are people of example, and what is ugly in the domain of seclusion may be good in the domain of example, and vice versa. And Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak said about him: 'There will always be in this nation someone by whom Allah revives their religion and their worldly affairs.' He was asked: 'Who is that today?' He said: 'Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Kufi.' And an example of that is the criticism of one who rides a horse, even though that is among the means of jihad, like horse racing and foot races. And like the criticism of some for joking, and it is a matter that the sharia has permitted as long as it is truthful, not false, unless it is something that frivolity provokes, causing one to act recklessly and without care.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of invalid jarh by listing specific examples of criticisms that do not constitute valid grounds for rejecting a narrator because the actions criticized are not sinful in sharia. The first example is the ignorant criticism of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan (Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani) based on a personal dispute with Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak, who refused to read hadith to him. The author argues that even if the story is true, it does not harm Imam Muhammad's reliability because the manners of fiqh scholars differ from those of ascetics; what is considered rude for a recluse may be acceptable for a leader who must be firm. Moreover, Ibn al-Mubarak himself praised Imam Muhammad highly. The second example is criticism for riding a horse, which is actually a means of jihad and therefore praiseworthy. The third example is criticism for joking; the author notes that joking is permitted in sharia as long as it is truthful, not false, and does not lead to reckless behavior. The passage emphasizes that such actions are not sins and thus cannot be used to impugn a narrator's reliability.

Key Terms
جاهل (jāhil): Someone who criticizes without knowledge or valid reason
قدوة (qudwah): A person who is an exemplar for others, especially scholars who must maintain discipline

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered a valid jarh according to this passage?
A) A narrator who jokes truthfully
B) A narrator who rides a horse for jihad preparation
C) A narrator who refuses to read hadith to someone
D) All of the above are invalid jarh

Key Takeaway
Invalid jarh is that which criticizes actions that are not sinful in sharia; valid jarh must be based on a clear sin.

Negation Supported by Evidence Equals Affirmation
Establishing a rule for when negation equals affirmation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that negation (nafi) supported by evidence (dalil) carries the same weight as affirmation (ithbat) in legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Negation backed by evidence is as strong as affirmation in Hanafi usul.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, negation (al-nafy) and affirmation (al-ithbat) often conflict. Early scholars applied different preferences across various cases, prompting the need for a unifying principle. This lesson establishes that when negation is supported by a clear evidence (dalil), it is treated like affirmation, no longer weaker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict
Recognize that different Hanafi rulings on negation versus affirmation (e.g., marriage of Maymuna, return of Zaynab, purity, jarh) show varied preferences, demanding a general rule.
This step sets the context for the principle.
2
Categorize negation
Understand the three categories: negation known by evidence, negation not known by evidence, and ambiguous negation.
The category determines the treatment.
3
Apply the rule
When negation is of the first type (known by evidence), it is equivalent to affirmation.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Analyze the example
Examine Imam Muhammad's example: a husband denies blasphemy by claiming he was quoting Christians. His additional phrase (evidence) makes his negation accepted.
The example illustrates the rule in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما اختلف عملهم : لم يكن بُدَّ من أصل جامع، وذلك أن نقول : إن النفي لا يخلو من أوجه: إما أن يكون مما يُعرف بدليله. أو لا يعرف بدليله. أو يشتبه حاله : فإن كان من جنس ما يُعرف بدليله : كان مثل الإثبات. وذلك مثل ما قال محمد رحمه الله في السير الكبير : في رجل ادعت عليه امرأته أنها سمعته يقول : المسيح ابن الله ، فقال الزوج: إنما قلتُ: المسيح ابنُ الله قول النصارى، أو قالت النصارى المسيح ابن الله، لكنها لم تسمع الزيادة فالقول قوله.
Since their practice differed, there had to be a comprehensive principle, and that is that we say: Negation is not free from aspects: either it is known by its evidence, or it is not known by its evidence, or its state is ambiguous. If it is of the type that is known by its evidence, it is like affirmation. That is like what Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said in al-Siyar al-Kabir: concerning a man whose wife claimed that she heard him say 'the Messiah is the son of God,' and the husband said, 'I only said: the Messiah is the son of God is the saying of the Christians,' or 'the Christians said the Messiah is the son of God,' but she did not hear the addition, so his statement is accepted.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh regarding the treatment of negation (nafi) when it conflicts with affirmation (ithbat). The author observes that early Hanafi scholars applied different preferences across various cases—sometimes favoring the negation (e.g., the marriage of the Prophet to Maymuna), sometimes the affirmation (e.g., the return of Zaynab), and in the case of law and purity, they favored purity (a type of negation). In witness credibility, they favored jarh (impeachment), which is a form of affirmation. To reconcile these varying practices, the author deduces a general rule: negation is categorized into three types based on the presence of a known evidence (dalil). If negation is supported by a clear evidence, it carries the same weight as affirmation. The example from Imam Muhammad's al-Siyar al-Kabir illustrates this: a husband denies uttering blasphemy by clarifying that he was quoting the Christians. His negation is backed by the additional phrase he claims to have said, which provides evidence for his claim. Since the wife did not hear the full statement, the husband's version is accepted. This shows that when negation is substantiated by a recognizable proof, it is treated like positive proof.

Key Terms
النفي (al-nafy): A statement denying a fact or ruling; the absence of something.
الإثبات (al-ithbat): A statement establishing a fact or ruling; asserting existence.
دليل (dalil): Evidence or proof that makes a ruling apparent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle in this passage, when does negation (al-nafy) carry the same weight as affirmation (al-ithbat)?
A) When it is based on a rational argument
B) When it is known by its evidence
C) When it is supported by Ijma
D) When it is the opinion of a companion

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, negation supported by clear evidence (dalil) is equivalent to affirmation.
